Three Conditions for a Valid Definition
Author lists and explains the second, third, and fourth conditions for a valid definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three conditions of a valid definition (prior knowledge, clarity, co-extensiveness) and detect violations in example definitions from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be prior known, clearer, and co-extensive with the defined.

In this passage, the author explains three essential conditions that any proper definition must satisfy. These conditions ensure that a definition actually clarifies the meaning of a term rather than obscuring it. We will examine each condition, see examples of violations, and understand how they safeguard the epistemological purpose of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
The passage explicitly lists three conditions: (2) the definer must be known before the defined, (3) the definer must be clearer than the defined, (4) the definer must be co-extensive with the defined. Locate the Arabic text for each.
2
Analyze the second condition: prior knowledge
The definer's components must already be known to the person being taught. The author compares this to a cause preceding its effect. Look at the prohibition of circular definitions (e.g., defining father by son and son by father) and the exception for simultaneously defining two unknowns.
This prevents endless regression or circularity.
3
Analyze the third condition: clarity
The definer must be more evident than the defined. The author gives examples: defining 'even' as 'not odd' when both are equally obscure is invalid, but valid if one knows 'even' as 'number divisible into two equal parts'. Also, defining a clearer thing by a more obscure thing (e.g., fire by soul) is forbidden.
This condition logically subsumes the second, but is stated separately.
4
Analyze the fourth condition: co-extensiveness
The definer and defined must apply to exactly the same set of things. The definer should not be broader or narrower. The author discusses this for both descriptive (rasm) and essential (hadd) definitions.
In essential definition, if the essentials are not more known, one cannot replace them; instead, one must move to a second defined until reaching self-evident terms.
5
Apply the conditions to an example
Take a definition from an Islamic science, such as 'Salah is speech and action beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' and check if it satisfies all three conditions: Is the definer (speech, action, takbir, taslim) prior known? Is it clearer than 'salah'? Is it co-extensive?
This tests your understanding of the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْرِفَةُ المُعَرِّفِ - بِالكَسْرِ - سَابِقَةٌ عَلَى مَعْرِفَةِ المُعَرَّفِ (٢) ؛ لِوُجُوبِ تَقَدُّم السَّبَبِ عَلَى مُسَيَّبِهِ، وَمَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ أَنْ تَكُونَ أَجْزَاءُ التَّعْرِيفِ مُتَصَوَّرَةٌ عِنْدَ المُعَرِّفِ لَهُ قَبْلَ التَّعْرِيفِ بِهَا ، فَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِمَجْهُولٍ ؛ لامْتِنَاعِ التَّوَصُّلِ بِمَجْهُول إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ إِلَّا إِذَا ذُكِرَ تَعْرِيفُ المَجْهُولِ مَعَهُ ؛ كَأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ لِمَنْ يَجْهَلُهُ، وَيَجْهَلُ الحَيَوَانِ»: «الإِنْسَانُ هُوَ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ ، وَ(٣) : «الحَيَوَانُ هُوَ : الجِسْمُ النَّامِي : الحَسَّاسُ، وَلِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ يَمْتَنِعُ تَعْرِيفُ الشَّيْء بِمَا تَتَوَقَّفُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ عَلَى مَعْرِفَةِ ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ كَالْمُتَضَايفَيْنِ كَـ : «الأُبُوَّةِ، وَالبُنُوَةِ» ; لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعْرَفُ أَحَدُهُمَا قَبْلَ الآخَرِ، بَلْ يُعْرَفَانِ مَعاً. الثَّالِثُ : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - أَجْلَى عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ - بِالفَتْحِ - أَيْ : أَظْهَرَ مِنْهُ وَأَوْضَحَ، فَلَا يُعَرَّفُ الشَّيْءُ بِمَا يُسَاوِيهِ فِي الخَفَاءِ كَتَعْرِيفِ الزَّوْجِ بِـ: «مَا لَيْسَ بِفَرْدٍ» وَ: «الفَرْدِ بِـ : «مَا لَيْسَ بِزَوْجِ» بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَنِ اسْتَوَيَا عِنْدَهُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْ عَرَفَ أَنَّ الزَّوْجَ هُوَ : «العَدَدُ المُنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ» , فَيَصِحُ أَنْ يُعَرَّفَ لَهُ الفَرْدُ بِـ : «مَا لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ» وَلَا بِمَا هُوَ أَخْفَى مِنْهُ كَتَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ بِـ : «أَنَّهَا جِسْمُ كَالنَّفْسِ»، فَإِنَّ النَّفْسَ أَخْفَى مِنَ النَّارِ، وَهَذَا الشَّرْطُ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا قَبْلَهُ، وَإِنَّمَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ تَبَعاً لِلْمُخْتَصَرِ . الرَّابِعُ : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ مُسَاوِياً لِلمُعَرَّفِ فِي الصِّدْقِ؛ أَيْ : أَنْ يَصْدُقَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ ، فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ ـ أَعَمَّ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ، وَلَا أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ. فإن قلت هذا الشَّرط في الرَّسم لكونه بالعَرَضيَّات ظاهر، وأما الحد فهل يعتبر فيه ذلك أم لا؟ قلت : نعم، لكن ليس معناه في الحد أنَّ ذاتيَّات الشَّيء إن لم تكن أعرف تركناها وجئنا بذاتيَّاتٍ أخرى أعرف منها ; لاستحالة ذلك، بل ينتقل إلى طلب المعرَّف الثاني إلى أن ينتهي الأمر إلى حيز البداهة . قوله : وَلَا بِمَا هُوَ أَخْفَى هذا أحرويٌّ ، فكان من حقه أن يقول : وبالأولى أن لا يعرف الشَّيء بما هو أخفى . قوله : (كَتَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ) بِـ أَنَّهَا جِسْمٌ كَالنَّفْسِ أي : في البساطة وعدم أجزاء متباينة، أو في أنَّها تحدث خفّة للجار كما أنَّ الجسم يخف بالنفس، كما قيل: ثَقُلَتْ زُجَاجَاتٌ أَتَتْنَا فُرَّعاً حَتَّى إِذَا مُلِئَتْ بِصَرْفِ الرَّاحِ خَفَّتْ وَكَادَتْ أَنْ تَطِيرَ بِمَا حَوَتْ وَكَذَا الجُسُومُ تَخِتُ بِالأَرْوَاحِ . قوله : (وَهَذَا الشَّرْطُ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا قَبْلَهُ) أي : لأنَّ شرط كونه سابقاً في المعرفة على المعرف يتفرع عنه أن لا يتعرَّف الشَّيء بمساويه في الجهل، فأحرى ما هو أخفى. وأجيب : بأنَّ أخذ الشَّيء تصريحاً أولى من أخذه التزاماً ، فالتّنصيص على الشروط مطابقة ليحافظ على جميعها أولى، لكنَّ الأولى أن لو قدَّم هذا الشَّرط؛ لأنَّ إغناء المتأخر عن المتقدم لا يضر، بخلاف العكس. قوله : (الرَّابِعُ) تبعَ في هذا التَّفريع السنوسي في شرح المختصر، وفي أخذه من الحد المذكور نظر ; لما تقدَّمَ: أنَّ هذا التَّعريف للمتقدمين، وهم يجيزون التّعريف بالأعم والأخص.
The second: It is necessary that the knowledge of the definer (with kasr) be prior to the knowledge of the defined (with fatha); due to the necessity of the cause preceding its effect. Its meaning: that the parts of the definition must be conceptualized by the one defining before defining with them. So definition by an unknown is invalid, because it is impossible to reach the unknown through an unknown, unless the definition of the unknown is mentioned along with it; such as if it is said in defining 'human' to one who does not know it and does not know 'animal': 'Human is the rational animal' and 'Animal is the growing, sentient body'. And because of this condition, defining a thing by that whose knowledge depends on the knowledge of that thing is prohibited, like correlatives, such as 'fatherhood' and 'sonship'; because neither is known before the other, rather they are known together. The third: It is necessary that the definer (with kasr) be more evident to the listener than the defined (with fatha), i.e., more apparent and clearer. So a thing is not defined by what is equal to it in obscurity, like defining 'even' by 'what is not odd' and 'odd' by 'what is not even' for one to whom both are equally obscure. But for one who knows that 'even' is 'the number divisible into two equal parts', it is valid to define 'odd' for him by 'what is not even'. Nor by what is more obscure than it, like defining 'fire' by 'it is a body like the soul', for the soul is more obscure than fire. And this condition dispenses with the previous one; we only mentioned it following the Mukhtasar. The fourth: It is necessary that the definer be co-extensive with the defined in truth; i.e., that each apply truly to everything the other applies to. So the definer (with kasr) should not be more general than the defined, nor more specific. If you say: This condition is apparent in the case of descriptive definition because it uses accidental properties; but in the case of essential definition, is it considered or not? I say: Yes, but its meaning in essential definition is not that if the essentials of a thing are not more known we leave them and bring other essentials that are more known—that is impossible. Rather, one moves to seek the second defined until the matter reaches the realm of self-evidence. His statement: 'Nor by what is more obscure' is a fortiori; so it should have said: 'A fortiori, a thing should not be defined by what is more obscure.' His statement: 'like defining fire by it is a body like the soul' meaning in simplicity and absence of disparate parts, or in that it produces lightness for the stomach just as the body becomes light with the soul, as it is said: 'Heavy glass vessels came to us, until when filled with pure wine they became light and almost flew with what they contained; and likewise bodies become light with souls.' His statement: 'And this condition dispenses with the previous one' meaning: because the condition of being prior in knowledge to the defined entails that a thing should not be defined by its equal in ignorance, so a fortiori not by what is more obscure. And it was answered: Taking the thing explicitly is better than taking it implicitly, so specifying the conditions by direct mention to preserve them all is better. But it would have been better if this condition had been presented earlier, because the later dispensing with the earlier does no harm, unlike the reverse. His statement: 'The fourth'—he followed in this subdivision al-Sanusi in his commentary on al-Mukhtasar; and there is consideration in taking it from the mentioned definition, because it has been stated that this definition is of the early scholars, and they permit definition by the more general and the more specific.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions of definition, specifically the second, third, and fourth conditions as outlined in al-Sanusi's Mukhtasar. The author (Bajuri/Bannani) explains each condition with examples and refines points from previous commentaries.

The second condition states that the definer's knowledge must precede the defined's knowledge. This is likened to a cause preceding its effect. The definer (the terms used in the definition) must be already known to the person defining before he uses them to define something else. Defining by an unknown term is invalid because an unknown cannot lead to knowledge of another unknown. An exception is made if the unknown term is also defined alongside, e.g., defining 'human' as 'rational animal' and simultaneously defining 'animal' as 'growing, sentient body' for someone ignorant of both. This condition also prohibits circular definitions, such as defining correlatives like 'father' and 'son' by each other, since neither is known before the other.

The third condition requires the definer to be clearer (ajla) than the defined. Thus a thing should not be defined by something equally obscure, e.g., defining 'even' as 'not odd' when both are equally unknown. However, if the definer is known, it is valid. Also, the definer must not be more obscure than the defined, e.g., defining 'fire' as 'a body like the soul', since the soul is more obscure. The commentary notes that this condition actually subsumes the second (since clarity implies prior knowledge), but the authors mention it separately following the Mukhtasar. There is a discussion about the ordering: explicit mention of conditions is better, though the third could have been placed earlier.

The fourth condition demands that the definer be co-extensive (musawin) with the defined, meaning they apply to exactly the same things. So the definer should not be broader (more general) or narrower (more specific) than the defined. This is obvious for descriptive definitions (rasm) using accidental properties, but for essential definitions (hadd), the condition also applies. However, if the essential properties are not more known, one cannot replace them with other essentials; rather, one moves to define the second definer until reaching self-evident terms. The commentary notes that al-Sanusi derived this condition from a definition used by earlier scholars who allowed definition by broader or narrower terms, so there is some inconsistency.

Throughout, the author engages in typical hashiya style: correcting, refining, and explaining the implications of the conditions. The examples from logic (human, animal, even, odd, fire, soul) are used to illustrate violations and valid cases.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a violation of the third condition (clarity)?
A) Defining 'human' as 'rational animal' for someone who knows both terms.
B) Defining 'even' as 'not odd' for someone to whom both are equally obscure.
C) Defining 'animal' as 'growing, sentient body' alongside defining 'human'.
D) Defining 'fire' as 'a body like the soul' for someone who knows the soul well.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must satisfy three conditions: the definer must be prior known, clearer than the defined, and co-extensive with it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Examine the definition: 'al-Salah is speech and action, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim.' Check whether each condition (prior knowledge, clarity, co-extensiveness) is satisfied. Identify any potential violation.
?
Task 2: Consider the definition of 'iman' as 'assent in the heart, utterance on the tongue, and action of the limbs'. Does this definition meet the third condition (clarity)? Is 'assent in the heart' more evident than 'iman' itself? Discuss.

Hadd and Rasm

The Fourth Condition: Coextensiveness
Commentary on the fourth condition of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test a definition for coextensiveness and distinguish how the condition applies to hadd versus rasm.

Core Idea
The definiens must be neither too broad nor too narrow; it must match the definiendum exactly.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth condition of a valid definition: coextensiveness (al-musāwāh). The definiens (mu'arraf) must apply to exactly the same things as the definiendum (mu'arraf). We will also see how this condition is satisfied differently in essential definitions (hadd) and descriptive definitions (rasm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate the term being defined (mu'arraf) and the defining phrase (mu'arraf) in a given definition.
2
Test for generality or specificity
Check if the definiens is more general (a'amm) or more specific (akhass) than the definiendum. If it is, the condition is violated.
For example, defining 'human' as 'animal' is too broad; defining it as 'rational animal with a book' is too narrow.
3
Determine if the definition is hadd or rasm
If the definition uses essential attributes (genus + differentia), it is a hadd. If it uses accidental properties (genus + property), it is a rasm. The condition of coextensiveness applies to both, but its meaning differs for hadd.
In hadd, coextensiveness is inherent; in rasm, it must be checked explicitly.
4
Ensure the definiens is muttarid and mun'akis
Muttarid means it excludes non-instances (not too broad); mun'akis means it includes all instances (not too narrow). Together they ensure coextensiveness.
The commentator stresses that the definiens must be neither more general nor more specific, and a fortiori cannot be both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ مُسَاوِياً لِلمُعَرَّفِ فِي الصِّدْقِ؛ أَيْ : أَنْ يَصْدُقَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ ، فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ ـ أَعَمَّ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ، وَلَا أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ.
فإن قلت هذا الشَّرط في الرَّسم لكونه بالعَرَضيَّات ظاهر، وأما الحد فهل يعتبر فيه ذلك أم لا؟ قلت : نعم، لكن ليس معناه في الحد أنَّ ذاتيَّات الشَّيء إن لم تكن أعرف تركناها وجئنا بذاتيَّاتٍ أخرى أعرف منها ؛ لاستحالة ذلك، بل ينتقل إلى طلب المعرَّف الثاني إلى أن ينتهي الأمر إلى حيز البداهة .
قوله : وَلَا بِمَا هُوَ أَخْفَى هذا أحرويٌّ ، فكان من حقه أن يقول : وبالأولى أن لا يعرف الشَّيء بما هو أخفى . قوله : (كَتَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ) بِـ أَنَّهَا جِسْمٌ كَالنَّفْسِ أي : في البساطة وعدم أجزاء متباينة، أو في أنَّها تحدث خفّة للجار كما أنَّ الجسم يخف بالنفس، كما قيل: ثَقُلَتْ زُجَاجَاتٌ أَتَتْنَا فُرَّعاً حَتَّى إِذَا مُلِئَتْ بِصَرْفِ الرَّاحِ خَفَّتْ وَكَادَتْ أَنْ تَطِيرَ بِمَا حَوَتْ وَكَذَا الجُسُومُ تَخِتُ بِالأَرْوَاحِ قوله : (وَهَذَا الشَّرْطُ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا قَبْلَهُ) أي : لأنَّ شرط كونه سابقاً في المعرفة على المعرف يتفرع عنه أن لا يتعرَّف الشَّيء بمساويه في الجهل، فأحرى ما هو أخفى. وأجيب : بأنَّ أخذ الشَّيء تصريحاً أولى من أخذه التزاماً ، فالتّنصيص على الشروط مطابقة ليحافظ على جميعها أولى، لكنَّ الأولى أن لو قدَّم هذا الشَّرط؛ لأنَّ إغناء المتأخر عن المتقدم لا يضر، بخلاف العكس.
قوله : (الرَّابِعُ) تبعَ في هذا التَّفريع السنوسي في شرح المختصر، وفي أخذه من الحد المذكور نظر ؛ لما تقدَّمَ: أنَّ هذا التَّعريف للمتقدمين، وهم يجيزون التّعريف بالأعم والأخص.
كَمَا لَا يَكُونُ مُبَايناً لَهُ بِالأَوْلَى، فَبِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ أَعَمِّ يَكُونُ مُظَرِداً ؛ أَي : مَانِعاً غَيْرَ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ فِي حَدِّهِ، وَبِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ أَخَصٌ يَكُونُ مُنْعَكِساً ؛ أَيْ: جَامِعاً لِجَمِيعِ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ، وَبِالأَوْلَى يَمْتَنِعُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَعَمَّ وَأَخَصَّ مِنْ وَجْهِ؛ لِفَسَادِ طَرْدِهِ وَعَكْسِهِ مَعاً، وَالمُبَايِنُ أَشَدُّ فَسَاداً ؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ مِنَ المَطْلُوبِ، وَيَأْتِي إِبْضَاحُ ذَلِكَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تَعَالَى.
The fourth: that it is necessary that the definiens is coextensive with the definiendum in truth; meaning: that each of them applies to everything the other applies to, so the definiens (with kasra) is not more general than the definiendum, nor more specific. If you say: This condition is apparent for the rasm because it is by accidents, but as for the hadd, is it considered there or not? I say: Yes, but its meaning in the hadd is not that if the essentials of a thing are not more known we abandon them and bring other essentials that are more known; that is impossible. Rather, one moves to seeking the second definition until it ends at the level of self-evidence. His saying: 'And not by what is more obscure than it' is a fortiori, so it would have been proper for him to say: 'And a fortiori a thing should not be defined by what is more obscure than it.' His saying: (like defining fire) as 'a body like the soul' meaning: in simplicity and absence of disparate parts, or in that it produces lightness for the neighbor just as the body becomes light by the soul, as was said: 'Heavy glass vessels came to us in rows, until when filled with pure wine they became light and almost flew with what they contained, and likewise bodies become light with souls.' His saying: (And this condition renders what preceded it unnecessary) meaning: because the condition of it being prior in knowledge to the definiendum entails that a thing should not be defined by what is equal to it in ignorance, let alone what is more obscure. And it was answered: that taking the thing explicitly is better than taking it implicitly, so specifying the conditions literally to preserve all of them is better, but it would have been better if he had presented this condition, because the later making the earlier unnecessary does no harm, contrary to the reverse. His saying: (The fourth) he followed in this branching Sanusi in his commentary on al-Mukhtasar, and in taking it from the mentioned definition there is consideration; because as previously mentioned, this definition is of the earlier ones, and they allowed definition by the more general and the more specific. Just as it must not be contradictory to it a fortiori, so by its not being more general it is universal (muttarid); i.e., preventing what is not the defined from entering into its definition, and by its not being more specific it is converse (mun'akis); i.e., encompassing all individuals of the defined. And a fortiori it is impossible that it be more general and more specific in one respect, because of the corruption of both its universality and converse. And the contradictory is more corrupt; because of its not applying to anything of the intended. And clarification of that will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two key conditions for a valid definition. The fourth condition is that the definiens (mu'arraf) must be coextensive (musawin) with the definiendum (mu'arraf) in truth, meaning they apply to exactly the same set of things. The definiens should neither be more general (a'amm) nor more specific (akhass). The commentator then addresses a question: this condition is obvious for descriptive definitions (rasm) which use accidental properties, but is it also required for essential definitions (hadd)? The answer is yes, but its application differs. In a hadd, if the essential attributes are not already known, one does not replace them with other essentials; rather, one continues to refine the definition until it reaches self-evident concepts. The second condition discussed is that the definiens must not be more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum. The commentator notes that this condition is actually a fortiori (ahrawi) and should logically supersede the previous condition (that the definiens be better known). He gives an example of defining fire as 'a body like the soul' – a poor definition because 'soul' is more obscure than fire. The commentary then examines whether the condition of not being more obscure renders the earlier condition (that the definiens be prior in knowledge) redundant. The answer is that explicit statement of all conditions is preferable to implication. The passage ends by elaborating the fourth condition further: the definiens must be universal (muttarid, i.e., exclude non-instances) and converse (mun'akis, i.e., include all instances). Any deficiency in these aspects makes the definition invalid, and a definition that is completely disjoint (mubayin) is even worse.

Avoiding Obscurity in Definitions
Commentary on the condition of not defining by something more obscure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a definition that uses a more obscure definiens and explain why the condition of not being more obscure is a fortiori.

Core Idea
The definiens must be better known than the definiendum; otherwise, the definition fails to clarify.

This lesson focuses on the condition that the definiens must not be more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum. The commentator explains that this condition is a fortiori (ahrawi) and discusses whether it renders the earlier condition (that the definiens be prior in knowledge) redundant. We will also examine the example of defining fire as 'a body like the soul' as a violation of this condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens in a definition
Determine which term is being defined and which is used to define it.
e.g., in 'fire is a body like the soul', fire is definiendum, 'body like the soul' is definiens.
2
Assess the relative clarity of the definiens
Is the definiens more obscure than the definiendum? If the definiens is less known or more difficult to understand, the condition is violated.
For example, 'soul' is more mysterious than fire.
3
Apply the a fortiori principle
If the definiens should not be equal in obscurity, then it certainly should not be more obscure. The commentator argues that the condition of being better known implies not being more obscure, so the explicit statement is still preferred.
This step involves reasoning from stronger to weaker.
4
Evaluate the redundancy argument
Does the condition of avoiding obscurity make the prior condition (better known) unnecessary? The commentator notes that explicit statement of all conditions is better, though the later condition could have been presented first.
This shows the interplay between conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ مُسَاوِياً لِلمُعَرَّفِ فِي الصِّدْقِ؛ أَيْ : أَنْ يَصْدُقَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ ، فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ ـ أَعَمَّ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ، وَلَا أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ.
فإن قلت هذا الشَّرط في الرَّسم لكونه بالعَرَضيَّات ظاهر، وأما الحد فهل يعتبر فيه ذلك أم لا؟ قلت : نعم، لكن ليس معناه في الحد أنَّ ذاتيَّات الشَّيء إن لم تكن أعرف تركناها وجئنا بذاتيَّاتٍ أخرى أعرف منها ؛ لاستحالة ذلك، بل ينتقل إلى طلب المعرَّف الثاني إلى أن ينتهي الأمر إلى حيز البداهة .
قوله : وَلَا بِمَا هُوَ أَخْفَى هذا أحرويٌّ ، فكان من حقه أن يقول : وبالأولى أن لا يعرف الشَّيء بما هو أخفى . قوله : (كَتَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ) بِـ أَنَّهَا جِسْمٌ كَالنَّفْسِ أي : في البساطة وعدم أجزاء متباينة، أو في أنَّها تحدث خفّة للجار كما أنَّ الجسم يخف بالنفس، كما قيل: ثَقُلَتْ زُجَاجَاتٌ أَتَتْنَا فُرَّعاً حَتَّى إِذَا مُلِئَتْ بِصَرْفِ الرَّاحِ خَفَّتْ وَكَادَتْ أَنْ تَطِيرَ بِمَا حَوَتْ وَكَذَا الجُسُومُ تَخِتُ بِالأَرْوَاحِ قوله : (وَهَذَا الشَّرْطُ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا قَبْلَهُ) أي : لأنَّ شرط كونه سابقاً في المعرفة على المعرف يتفرع عنه أن لا يتعرَّف الشَّيء بمساويه في الجهل، فأحرى ما هو أخفى. وأجيب : بأنَّ أخذ الشَّيء تصريحاً أولى من أخذه التزاماً ، فالتّنصيص على الشروط مطابقة ليحافظ على جميعها أولى، لكنَّ الأولى أن لو قدَّم هذا الشَّرط؛ لأنَّ إغناء المتأخر عن المتقدم لا يضر، بخلاف العكس.
قوله : (الرَّابِعُ) تبعَ في هذا التَّفريع السنوسي في شرح المختصر، وفي أخذه من الحد المذكور نظر ؛ لما تقدَّمَ: أنَّ هذا التَّعريف للمتقدمين، وهم يجيزون التّعريف بالأعم والأخص.
كَمَا لَا يَكُونُ مُبَايناً لَهُ بِالأَوْلَى، فَبِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ أَعَمِّ يَكُونُ مُظَرِداً ؛ أَي : مَانِعاً غَيْرَ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ فِي حَدِّهِ، وَبِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ أَخَصٌ يَكُونُ مُنْعَكِساً ؛ أَيْ: جَامِعاً لِجَمِيعِ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ، وَبِالأَوْلَى يَمْتَنِعُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَعَمَّ وَأَخَصَّ مِنْ وَجْهِ؛ لِفَسَادِ طَرْدِهِ وَعَكْسِهِ مَعاً، وَالمُبَايِنُ أَشَدُّ فَسَاداً ؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ مِنَ المَطْلُوبِ، وَيَأْتِي إِبْضَاحُ ذَلِكَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تَعَالَى.
The fourth: that it is necessary that the definiens is coextensive with the definiendum in truth; meaning: that each of them applies to everything the other applies to, so the definiens (with kasra) is not more general than the definiendum, nor more specific. If you say: This condition is apparent for the rasm because it is by accidents, but as for the hadd, is it considered there or not? I say: Yes, but its meaning in the hadd is not that if the essentials of a thing are not more known we abandon them and bring other essentials that are more known; that is impossible. Rather, one moves to seeking the second definition until it ends at the level of self-evidence. His saying: 'And not by what is more obscure than it' is a fortiori, so it would have been proper for him to say: 'And a fortiori a thing should not be defined by what is more obscure than it.' His saying: (like defining fire) as 'a body like the soul' meaning: in simplicity and absence of disparate parts, or in that it produces lightness for the neighbor just as the body becomes light by the soul, as was said: 'Heavy glass vessels came to us in rows, until when filled with pure wine they became light and almost flew with what they contained, and likewise bodies become light with souls.' His saying: (And this condition renders what preceded it unnecessary) meaning: because the condition of it being prior in knowledge to the definiendum entails that a thing should not be defined by what is equal to it in ignorance, let alone what is more obscure. And it was answered: that taking the thing explicitly is better than taking it implicitly, so specifying the conditions literally to preserve all of them is better, but it would have been better if he had presented this condition, because the later making the earlier unnecessary does no harm, contrary to the reverse. His saying: (The fourth) he followed in this branching Sanusi in his commentary on al-Mukhtasar, and in taking it from the mentioned definition there is consideration; because as previously mentioned, this definition is of the earlier ones, and they allowed definition by the more general and the more specific. Just as it must not be contradictory to it a fortiori, so by its not being more general it is universal (muttarid); i.e., preventing what is not the defined from entering into its definition, and by its not being more specific it is converse (mun'akis); i.e., encompassing all individuals of the defined. And a fortiori it is impossible that it be more general and more specific in one respect, because of the corruption of both its universality and converse. And the contradictory is more corrupt; because of its not applying to anything of the intended. And clarification of that will come, God willing.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two key conditions for a valid definition. The fourth condition is that the definiens (mu'arraf) must be coextensive (musawin) with the definiendum (mu'arraf) in truth, meaning they apply to exactly the same set of things. The definiens should neither be more general (a'amm) nor more specific (akhass). The commentator then addresses a question: this condition is obvious for descriptive definitions (rasm) which use accidental properties, but is it also required for essential definitions (hadd)? The answer is yes, but its application differs. In a hadd, if the essential attributes are not already known, one does not replace them with other essentials; rather, one continues to refine the definition until it reaches self-evident concepts. The second condition discussed is that the definiens must not be more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum. The commentator notes that this condition is actually a fortiori (ahrawi) and should logically supersede the previous condition (that the definiens be better known). He gives an example of defining fire as 'a body like the soul' – a poor definition because 'soul' is more obscure than fire. The commentary then examines whether the condition of not being more obscure renders the earlier condition (that the definiens be prior in knowledge) redundant. The answer is that explicit statement of all conditions is preferable to implication. The passage ends by elaborating the fourth condition further: the definiens must be universal (muttarid, i.e., exclude non-instances) and converse (mun'akis, i.e., include all instances). Any deficiency in these aspects makes the definition invalid, and a definition that is completely disjoint (mubayin) is even worse.

Division of Definitions: Hadd, Rasm, and Lafzi
Commentary classifies definitions into three main types with subtypes, totaling seven, and defines hadd tamm and rasm tamm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions into seven types and identify the components of a complete hadd and a complete rasm.

Core Idea
A definition is either essential, descriptive, or verbal, with complete and incomplete subtypes.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the poet's three-fold division of definitions, revealing a more nuanced classification of seven types. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for evaluating definitions in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary types
From the poem line, note the three types: hadd (essential), rasm (descriptive), and lafzi (verbal). The commentator confirms this as a starting point.
2
Expand to five
Each of hadd and rasm can be complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis), making five when combined with lafzi.
3
Add two more types
The commentator adds definition by example (ta'rif bi-l-mithal) and definition by division (ta'rif bi-l-qisma), totaling seven.
4
Understand the logical basis
The restriction to three is justified: definition is either by essential attributes (hadd) or not; if not, it is either a real compound (rasm) or merely verbal (lafzi).
5
Examine components of complete hadd and rasm
Complete hadd consists of proximate genus (jins qarib) and differentia (fasl). Complete rasm consists of genus and property (khassa).
The commentator notes that the poet used 'rasmi' with ya' of attribution for metrical reasons.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٤٠) مُعَرِّفٌ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ قُسِمْ حَدّ وَرَسْمِيٌّ وَلَفْظِيٌّ عُلِم. قَسَّمَ المُعَرِّفَ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ ، وَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ هِيَ خَمْسَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ تَامّ وَنَاقِصٌ، وَالرَّسْمَ تَامٌّ وَنَاقِصٌ ، وَالخَامِسُ المُعَرِّفُ اللَّفْظِيُّ. وَبَقِيَ عَلَيْهِ قِسْمَانِ آخَرَانِ هُمَا : التَّعْرِيفُ بِالمِثَالِ، وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالقِسْمَةِ ؛ فَصَارَتِ الأَقْسَامُ سَبْعَةٌ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مُعَرِّفٌ) مُبْتَدَأَ ، وَسَوَّعَ الِابْتِدَاءَ بِهِ قَصْدُ الجِنْسِ. (٤١) فَـ «الحَدُّ بِـ : الجِنْسِ وَفَصْلِ وَقَعَا وَالرَّسْمُ بِ: الجِنْسِ وَخَاصَةٍ مَعَا. وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الحَدِّ التَّامِّ بِقَوْلِهِ : (فَـ «الحَدُّ بِـ : الجِنْسِ وَفَصْلٍ وَقَعَا) يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الحَدَّ التَّامَّ هوَ: «مَا كَانَ بِالجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ وَالفَصْلِ، فَحَذَفَ الوَصْفَ فِي الحَدِّ وَالجِنْسِ ؛ لِدَلَالَةِ مَا ذَكَرَ فِي النَّاقِصِ بَعْدَهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَوَاءٌ ذُكِرَتْ فِيهِ أَجْزَاءُ الجِنْسِ مُفَصَّلَةٌ كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ». قول النَّاظم : مُعَرِّفْ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ ... إلخ) وَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ التَّعْرِيفَ إِمَّا بِمُجَرَّدِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ أَوْ لَا ، الأَوَّلُ : الحَدُّ ، وَالثَّانِي : إِمَّا أَنْ يَصِحَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَكَّباً أَوْ لَا، الأَوَّلُ: الرَّسْمُ، وَالثَّانِي : اللَّفْظِيُّ . قوله : (وَرَسْمِيُّ) الجاري على الألسنة إسقاط «ياء» النَّسب ، وزاد النَّاظمُ «ياء» النَّسب ؛ نظراً إلى أن المقسم وهو مطلق المعرف جنس ، فيصح أن يُنسب إلى بعض أنواعه ؛ كما يُقال: «الحَيَوَانُ : إِمَّا إِنْسَانِيٌّ ، أَوْ فَرَسِيٌّ» ؛ أي : هذا الجنس ينقسم إلى : حصَّةٍ إنسانية، وحصّةٍ فرسيَّةٍ، ولا يخفى ما فيه مِنَ التَكلُّف، أحوج إليه ضيقُ النّظم .
His saying: (40) 'A definiens is divided into three: hadd, rasmi, and lafzi, it is known.' He divided the definiens into three parts, and in reality they are five; because the hadd is complete and incomplete, and the rasm is complete and incomplete, and the fifth is the verbal definiens. And two other divisions remain: definition by example and definition by division; so the divisions become seven. His saying: (Definiens) is a subject, and beginning with it is permitted for the purpose of genus. (41) 'So the hadd is by genus and differentia, it occurs, and the rasm is by genus and property together.' And he pointed to the complete hadd by his saying: (So the hadd is by genus and differentia, it occurs) meaning: the complete hadd is that which is by the proximate genus and differentia, so he omitted the description in the hadd and the genus, because what he mentioned in the incomplete later indicates it, and it is the same whether the parts of the genus are mentioned in detail like the definition of 'human'. The author's saying: 'A definiens is divided into three...' etc.) The reason for the restriction is that definition is either by essential attributes alone or not; the first is the hadd, and the second is either valid as a compound or not; the first is the rasm, and the second is the verbal. His saying: (rasmi) the common practice on tongues is to drop the 'ya' of attribution, and the author added the 'ya' of attribution, considering that the divided thing, which is the absolute definiens, is a genus, so it is valid to attribute it to some of its types; as is said: 'The animal is either human-like or horse-like'; i.e., this genus is divided into a human portion and a horse portion, and the effort in it is not hidden, the tightness of the meter necessitated it.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage begins a new section classifying definitions. The commentator explains that the definiens (mu'arraf) is divided into three types: essential definition (hadd), descriptive definition (rasm), and verbal definition (lafzi). However, in reality there are five because each of hadd and rasm can be complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis), plus the verbal definition. Two more types are added: definition by example (ta'rif bi-l-mithal) and definition by division (ta'rif bi-l-qisma), making a total of seven. The commentator then explains that the word 'mu'arraf' is made the subject of the sentence because it is the genus being divided. Next, the commentator elaborates on the complete hadd: it is composed of the proximate genus (jins qarib) and the differentia (fasl). The phrase 'by genus and differentia' implies the complete hadd, and the incomplete hadd will be mentioned later. The same applies to rasm, which is by genus and property (khassa). The commentator then gives a logical reason for the restriction to three: definition is either by essential attributes (hadd) or not; if not, it is either a real compound (rasm) or just a verbal explanation. Finally, there is a linguistic note: the author used 'rasmi' with the ya' of attribution (nisha) to indicate that the generic category 'mu'arraf' is being divided into types, even though common usage drops the ya'. The poet resorted to this due to metrical constraints.

Hadd and Rasm

Types of Definitions: From Three to Seven
Commentary expands the threefold division of definitions to seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions as hadd (complete), rasm (descriptive), or lafdhi (verbal), and distinguish between perfect and imperfect forms.

Core Idea
Definitions come in seven types, not just three: perfect/imperfect hadd, perfect/imperfect rasm, lafdhi, by example, and by division.

The matn states that the definer is divided into three types. However, as the commentary explains, this simple division expands into five, then seven types when we consider perfection and imperfection, and additional methods like example and division. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for evaluating definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Determine what is being defined. This is the term or concept whose definition is being examined.
2
Check for essential attributes only
If the definition uses only essential attributes (dhatiyyat), it is a hadd. If it includes non-essential or accidental attributes, it falls under rasm or lafdhi.
Essential attributes are those that constitute the essence of the thing.
3
Determine compositeness
If the definition is composite (contains multiple attributes), it is a rasm. If it is simple (e.g., a synonym), it is a lafdhi definition.
Lafdhi definitions are merely verbal clarifications, not real definitions.
4
Assess perfection for hadd and rasm
For hadd and rasm, check whether the definition includes all essential parts (tamm/perfect) or only some (naqis/imperfect).
Perfect hadd includes all dhatiyyat; imperfect hadd omits some.
5
Recognize additional methods
The commentary adds two more types: definition by example (like 'water is this liquid') and definition by division (like 'animal is either human or horse').
These bring the total to seven.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٤٠) مُعَرِّفٌ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ قُسِمْ حَدٌ، وَرَسْمِيٌّ، وَلَفْظِيٌّ عُلِم. قَسَّمَ المُعَرِّفَ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ، وَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ هِيَ خَمْسَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ تَامّ وَنَاقِصٌ، وَالرَّسْمَ تَامُّ وَنَاقِصُ، وَالخَامِسُ المُعَرِّفُ اللَّفْظِيُّ. وَبَقِيَ عَلَيْهِ قِسْمَانِ آخَرَانِ هُمَا : التَّعْرِيفُ بِالمِثَالِ، وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالقِسْمَةِ؛ فَصَارَتِ الأَقْسَامُ سَبْعَةٌ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مُعَرِّفٌ) مُبْتَدَأَ، وَسَوَّعَ الِابْتَدَاءَ بِهِ قَصْدُ الجِنْسِ. قول النَّاظم : مُعَرِّفْ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ ... إلخ) وَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ التَّعْرِيفَ إِمَّا بِمُجَرَّدِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ أَوْ لا، الأَوَّلُ : الحَدُّ، وَالثَّانِي : إِمَّا أَنْ يَصِحَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَكَّباً أَوْ لا، الأَوَّلُ : الرَّسْمُ، وَالثَّانِي : اللَّفْظِيُّ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَرَسْمِيُّ) الجَارِي عَلَى الأَلْسِنَةِ إِسْقَاطُ «يَاءِ» النَّسَبِ، وَزَادَ النَّاظِمُ «يَاءَ» النَّسَبِ؛ نَظَراً إِلَى أَنَّ المُقَسَّمَ وَهُوَ مُطْلَقُ المُعَرِّفِ جِنْسٌ، فَيَصِحُّ أَنْ يُنْسَبَ إِلَى بَعْضِ أَنْوَاعِهِ؛ كَمَا يُقَالُ: «الحَيَوَانُ : إِمَّا إِنْسَانِيٌّ، أَوْ فَرَسِيٌّ»؛ أَيْ : هَذَا الجِنْسُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : حِصَّةٍ إِنْسَانِيَّةٍ، وَحِصَّةٍ فَرَسِيَّةٍ، وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَكَلُّفِ، أَحْوَجَ إِلَيْهِ ضِيقُ النَّظْمِ.
He said: 'A definer is divided into three: complete definition, descriptive definition, and verbal definition.' He divided the definer into three divisions, but in reality they are five, because the complete definition is either perfect or imperfect, and the descriptive definition is either perfect or imperfect, and the fifth is the verbal definer. Two other divisions remain: definition by example and definition by division; thus the divisions become seven. And his saying: 'A definer' is a subject, and beginning with it is permitted due to intending the genus. The author's saying: 'A definer into three...' etc. The reason for the restriction is that definition is either by mere essential attributes or not; the first is the complete definition, and the second: either it can be composite or not; the first is the descriptive definition, and the second is the verbal one. His saying: 'descriptive' – the common practice is to drop the relative 'ya', but the author added the relative 'ya' considering that the divided thing, i.e., the absolute definer, is a genus, so it is permissible to attribute it to some of its types, as it is said: 'animal: either human or horse', meaning this genus is divided into a human portion and a horse portion. This is not without artificiality, but the constraint of poetry necessitated it.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that states the definer is divided into three types: hadd, rasm, and lafdhi. The commentator immediately expands this, explaining that in reality there are five because hadd can be perfect or imperfect, and likewise rasm, with the fifth being the verbal definer. He then adds two more types: definition by example and definition by division, making the total seven. The discussion proceeds to justify the division logically: definition either employs only essential attributes (hadd), or not; if not, it either is composite (rasm) or simple (lafdhi). The commentator also addresses a grammatical nuance: the author added a 'ya' of relation to 'rasm' (making it 'rasmiyy'), whereas common usage drops it; this is allowed because the definer as a genus can be attributed to its species. An example from animal classification is given: 'animal is either human or horse'. The commentator acknowledges that this grammatical choice is somewhat artificial but necessary due to the constraints of poetry.

Key Terms
حَدّ (hadd): A definition that includes essential attributes (dhatiyyat) of the definiendum.
رَسْم (rasm): A definition that includes non-essential attributes (such as proper accidents) or a combination of essential and non-essential.
لَفْظِي (lafdhī): A verbal definition that clarifies the meaning of a word by synonym or description, not by real definition.
تَامّ (tāmm): Perfect or complete, referring to a definition that includes all essential parts of the definiendum.
نَاقِص (nāqiṣ): Imperfect or incomplete, referring to a definition that includes only some essential parts.
ذاتيّات (dhātiyyāt): Essential attributes that constitute the essence of a thing, as opposed to accidental attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, how many total types of definitions are there after adding definition by example and division?
A) 3
B) 5
C) 7
D) 9

Key Takeaway
The initial division of definitions into three types is expanded to seven by considering perfection/imperfection and additional methods like definition by example and by division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition of 'ijma'' (consensus) in a standard usul al-fiqh text. Determine whether it is a hadd or a rasm, and whether it is perfect or imperfect.
?
Given the definition: 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage made from grapes.' Classify it according to the five/seven types. Is it a hadd, rasm, or lafdhi? Is it perfect or imperfect?

The Perfect Definition and Its Essential Components
Definition of perfect definition with components and etymology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition includes all essential attributes and classify it as mutabaqa or tadammun.

Core Idea
A perfect definition includes all essential attributes; concomitant properties alone are insufficient.

In this passage, al-Bannani explains what makes a definition 'perfect' (tamm). He clarifies that a perfect definition must capture the entire essence of the definiendum by including all its essential attributes (dhatiyyat). This can be done either by listing them in explicit detail (mutabaqa) or by summarizing them through genus and differentia (tadammun). Importantly, defining by a mere concomitant property (iltizam) does not suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage. Here, it is 'human' (al-insan).
2
Extract the essential attributes
List all dhatiyyat that appear: body, growth, sensation, voluntary movement, rationality. These constitute the quiddity.
3
Determine the mode of expression
Check if the definition gives the parts in detail (mutabaqa) or summarily (tadammun). The example 'rational animal' is tadammun; the full list is mutabaqa.
4
Check for concomitant indication
Verify that no definition relies solely on a concomitant property (iltizam), like defining human as 'rational' alone. That is insufficient.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَرْحُ البَنَانِيِّ عَلَى السُّلَّمِ المُرَوَّنَقِ : «أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ النَّاطِقُ، أَوْ مُجْمَلَةٌ [ب/ ٢٦] ب: كَتَعْرِيفِهِ (۱) بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ». وَبِالجُمْلَةِ الحَدُّ التَّامُّ هُوَ المُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزَامِ. وَسُمِّيَ : «حَدًا»؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ»، وَهُوَ يَمْنَعُ غَيْرَ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ : فِيهِ، وَيَمْنَعُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الخُرُوجِ عَنْهُ . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا»؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِكَمَالِهَا . قَصَّارَةٌ قَوْلُهُ : (الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ إِنَّمَا جَمَعَ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي تَعْرِيفِ الحَيَوَانِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ أَحَدُهُمَا كَافٍ فِي إِخْرَاجِ غَيْرِ الحَيَوَانِ) مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ النَّامِي؛ تَنْبِيهاً عَلَى تَرَكُّبِ مَاهِيَّةِ «الحَيَوَانِ» مِنْهُمَا، زِيَادَةً عَلَى الجِسْمِيَّةِ وَالتُّمُوِّ، فَيَمْتَازُ عَنْ مُشَارِكِهِ فِيمَا قَبْلَ بِهَاتَيْنِ القُوَّتَيْنِ وَهُمَا المُدْرِكَةُ وَالمُتَحَرِّكَةُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا كَافِيَةً فِي إِخْرَاجِ غَيْرِ «الحَيَوَانِ» مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ النَّامِي، فَقُيُودُ الحُدُودِ يُرَادُ مِنْهَا بَيَانُ المَاهِيَةِ، وَالإِخْرَاجُ تَابِعٌ فَلَا يَضُرُّ تَخَلُّفُهُ. قَوْلُهُ : (بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ) المُطَابَقَةُ : ذِكْرُ أَجْزَاءِ الجِنْسِ مُفَصَّلَةً كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي ... إلخ»، وَالتَّضَمُّنُ : ذِكْرُ الأَجْزَاءِ مُجْمَلَةً كَتَعْرِيفِهِ بـ : الحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ». قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزَامِ) أَيْ: كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ «النَّاطِقِ»، فَلَا يُسَمَّى : حَدًّا تَامًّا. قَوْلُهُ : (لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ») مِنْهُ : الحَدِيدُ؛ سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَمْنَعُ مِنَ الأَعْدَاءِ، وَيُقَالُ: حَدَّتِ المَرْأَةُ عَلَى زَوْجِهَا وَأَحَدَّتْ إِذَا مَنَعَتْ نَفْسَهَا مِنَ الزِّينَةِ، وَحُدُودُ اللهِ تَعَالَى : مَا مَنَعَ مِنْهَا، أَوْ مَنْعُ مِنْ مُخَالَفَتِهَا وَالتَّعَدِّي إِلَى غَيْرِهَا؛ قَالَ اللهُ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَتِلْكَ حُدُودُ اللَّهِ فَلَا تَقْرَبُوهَا﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۷] . قَوْلُهُ : (وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا» ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ فِي تَمَامِ الحَدِّ وُجُوبُ التَّرْتِيبِ، بِأَنْ يُقَدَّمَ الجِنْسُ عِنْدَ إِرَادَةِ الحَدِّ وَالرَّسْمِ التَّامَّيْنِ عَلَى المُمَيِّزِ فَصْلاً أَوْ خَاصَّةً، وَفِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ لِلسَّنُوسِيِّ : وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ شَرَطَ فِي تَمَامِهِ التَّرْتِيبَ بِذِكْرِ الجُزْءِ الأَعَمِّ مُقَدَّماً عَلَى ذِكْرِ الجُزْءِ الأَخَصِّ، فَإِنْ عُكِسَ هَذَا التَّرْتِيبُ لَمْ يُسَمَّ عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ حَدًّا تَامًّا، بَلْ نَاقِصاً . اهـ . وَالمُشْتَرِطُ لِذَلِكَ هُوَ السَّرَّاجُ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ قَوْلِ ابْنِ عَرَفَةَ : وَيُقَدَّمُ الأَعَمُّ لِكَوْنِهِ أَعْرَفَ، وَفِي كَوْنِهِ وَاجِباً أَوْ أَوْلَى قَوْلَا السَّرَّاجِ وَالأَثِيرِ مَعَ الخُونَجِيِّ. اهـ .
The Bannani commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq: 'That it is the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body, or summarily as its definition by: 'that it is the rational animal'. In summary, the perfect definition is that which includes all essential attributes by direct reference or by implication, and no attention is paid to concomitant indication. It is called 'hadd' because hadd linguistically means 'prevention', and it prevents what is not among the individuals of the defined from entering into it, and prevents what is among the individuals of the defined from leaving it. It is called 'perfect' because it includes all the essential attributes completely. Marginal comment: His saying: 'sensing, voluntarily moving' – he combined both in defining 'animal', although one of them is sufficient to exclude what is not animal from among the types of growing things, as a reminder of the composition of the essence of 'animal' from both, in addition to corporeality and growth, so that it is distinguished from what shares with it in what precedes by these two powers, namely the perceiving and moving powers, even if one of them suffices to exclude what is not animal from among the types of growing things. For the conditions of definitions are intended to elucidate the essence, and exclusion is subsidiary, so its non-occurrence does not harm. His saying: 'by direct reference or by implication' – direct reference is mentioning the parts of the genus in detail, like defining 'human' as 'the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body', and implication is mentioning the parts summarily, like defining it as 'rational animal'. His saying: 'and no attention is paid to concomitant indication' means, like defining 'human' as 'rational', so it is not called a perfect definition. By his saying: 'because hadd linguistically means prevention' – from it is 'iron' (hadid), named so because it prevents enemies; and it is said: a woman observes 'iddah' (haddat) after her husband's death if she prevents herself from adornment; and the limits of Allah are what He has forbidden, or what prevents from violating them and transgressing to others; Allah says: 'These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them' (2:187). His saying: 'and it is called perfect' – its apparent meaning is that order is not necessary for the perfection of the definition, i.e., that one should precede the genus before the differentia or proper accident when intending the perfect definition and perfect description. In the commentary on al-Mukhtasar by al-Sanusi: 'Some of them have stipulated order in its perfection by mentioning the more general part before the more specific part; if this order is reversed, it is not called a perfect definition by them, but rather an imperfect one.' The one who stipulated that is al-Sarraj, as can be inferred from Ibn 'Arafa's statement: 'The more general is presented because it is more known, and whether it is obligatory or preferable are two opinions from al-Sarraj and al-Athir along with al-Khunaji.'
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the perfect definition (al-hadd al-tamm). It begins by giving an example of a perfect definition: 'human' defined in full as 'the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body', or summarized as 'rational animal'. The key point is that a perfect definition includes all essential attributes of the definiendum, either by direct reference (mutabaqa) where the parts of the genus are listed in detail, or by implication (tadammun) where they are mentioned summarily. Concomitant indication (iltizam) is not sufficient; a definition by a single property like 'rational' for human is not perfect. The passage then explains the etymology of 'hadd' as 'prevention', illustrating with linguistic examples: iron (hadid) prevents enemies, a woman in 'iddah prevents herself from adornment, and the limits of Allah prevent transgression. This etymology reflects the logical function of a definition: it prevents non-members from entering and members from leaving. The reason for calling it 'perfect' (tamm) is its complete inclusion of essential attributes. A marginal note clarifies why both 'sensing' and 'voluntarily moving' are included in the definition of animal, even though one suffices for exclusion: the purpose is to exposit the composite essence of animal, not merely to exclude. Exclusion is subsidiary. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether ordering (tarteeb) – presenting the genus before the differentia or property – is required for a perfect definition. The apparent meaning of the text is that it is not required, but the commentator notes that some scholars like al-Sarraj stipulated it; their opinion is cited from al-Sanusi's commentary.

مُطابَقَة
تَضَمُّن
اِلْتِزَام
ذاتيّات

According to al-Bannani's commentary, which type of indication is sufficient for a perfect definition?

B) Mutabaqa only
C) Tadammun only
D) Both mutabaqa and tadammun

Key Takeaway
A perfect definition captures all essential attributes of a thing, either in detail or summary, and a mere concomitant property does not qualify.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the example of 'salat', identify its essential attributes (dhatiyyat) according to the Hanafi school (e.g., takbir, qiyam, ruku', sujud, taslim). Then construct a perfect definition using either mutabaqa (list all in detail) or tadammun (summarize as 'a set of actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim').

The Condition of Order in Definitions: Tartib
Debate on ordering genus before differentia in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the scholarly disagreement on whether ordering genus before differentia is required for a perfect definition.

Core Idea
Some scholars require order in definitions; others deem it non-essential.

This passage continues the discussion of the perfect definition by examining whether it requires a specific order (tartib). The apparent meaning of the text suggests order is not necessary, but the commentator notes that some scholars, such as al-Sarraj, stipulate that the genus must precede the differentia. If reversed, the definition is considered imperfect by those scholars. The passage cites al-Sanusi and Ibn 'Arafa, showing a range of views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default position
The apparent meaning of the text is that order is not required for perfection. Note this as the starting point.
2
Extract the opposing view
Some scholars, like al-Sarraj, stipulate that the more general part (genus) must precede the more specific part (differentia or property). If reversed, the definition is considered imperfect.
3
Understand the rationale
The rationale cited from Ibn 'Arafa is that the more general is more known, so presenting it first aids clarity.
4
Distinguish obligatory vs. preferable
The passage notes two opinions: whether order is wajib (obligatory) or awla (preferable). Identify which scholars held which view.
Al-Sarraj and al-Athir have different stances, with al-Khunaji also mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَرْحُ البَنَانِيِّ عَلَى السُّلَّمِ المُرَوَّنَقِ : «أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ النَّاطِقُ، أَوْ مُجْمَلَةٌ [ب/ ٢٦] ب: كَتَعْرِيفِهِ (۱) بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ». وَبِالجُمْلَةِ الحَدُّ التَّامُّ هُوَ المُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزَامِ. وَسُمِّيَ : «حَدًا»؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ»، وَهُوَ يَمْنَعُ غَيْرَ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ : فِيهِ، وَيَمْنَعُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الخُرُوجِ عَنْهُ . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا»؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِكَمَالِهَا . قَصَّارَةٌ قَوْلُهُ : (الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ إِنَّمَا جَمَعَ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي تَعْرِيفِ الحَيَوَانِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ أَحَدُهُمَا كَافٍ فِي إِخْرَاجِ غَيْرِ الحَيَوَانِ) مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ النَّامِي؛ تَنْبِيهاً عَلَى تَرَكُّبِ مَاهِيَّةِ «الحَيَوَانِ» مِنْهُمَا، زِيَادَةً عَلَى الجِسْمِيَّةِ وَالتُّمُوِّ، فَيَمْتَازُ عَنْ مُشَارِكِهِ فِيمَا قَبْلَ بِهَاتَيْنِ القُوَّتَيْنِ وَهُمَا المُدْرِكَةُ وَالمُتَحَرِّكَةُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا كَافِيَةً فِي إِخْرَاجِ غَيْرِ «الحَيَوَانِ» مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ النَّامِي، فَقُيُودُ الحُدُودِ يُرَادُ مِنْهَا بَيَانُ المَاهِيَةِ، وَالإِخْرَاجُ تَابِعٌ فَلَا يَضُرُّ تَخَلُّفُهُ. قَوْلُهُ : (بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ) المُطَابَقَةُ : ذِكْرُ أَجْزَاءِ الجِنْسِ مُفَصَّلَةً كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي ... إلخ»، وَالتَّضَمُّنُ : ذِكْرُ الأَجْزَاءِ مُجْمَلَةً كَتَعْرِيفِهِ بـ : الحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ». قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزَامِ) أَيْ: كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ «النَّاطِقِ»، فَلَا يُسَمَّى : حَدًّا تَامًّا. قَوْلُهُ : (لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ») مِنْهُ : الحَدِيدُ؛ سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَمْنَعُ مِنَ الأَعْدَاءِ، وَيُقَالُ: حَدَّتِ المَرْأَةُ عَلَى زَوْجِهَا وَأَحَدَّتْ إِذَا مَنَعَتْ نَفْسَهَا مِنَ الزِّينَةِ، وَحُدُودُ اللهِ تَعَالَى : مَا مَنَعَ مِنْهَا، أَوْ مَنْعُ مِنْ مُخَالَفَتِهَا وَالتَّعَدِّي إِلَى غَيْرِهَا؛ قَالَ اللهُ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَتِلْكَ حُدُودُ اللَّهِ فَلَا تَقْرَبُوهَا﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۷] . قَوْلُهُ : (وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا» ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ فِي تَمَامِ الحَدِّ وُجُوبُ التَّرْتِيبِ، بِأَنْ يُقَدَّمَ الجِنْسُ عِنْدَ إِرَادَةِ الحَدِّ وَالرَّسْمِ التَّامَّيْنِ عَلَى المُمَيِّزِ فَصْلاً أَوْ خَاصَّةً، وَفِي شَرْحِ المُخْتَصَرِ لِلسَّنُوسِيِّ : وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ شَرَطَ فِي تَمَامِهِ التَّرْتِيبَ بِذِكْرِ الجُزْءِ الأَعَمِّ مُقَدَّماً عَلَى ذِكْرِ الجُزْءِ الأَخَصِّ، فَإِنْ عُكِسَ هَذَا التَّرْتِيبُ لَمْ يُسَمَّ عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ حَدًّا تَامًّا، بَلْ نَاقِصاً . اهـ . وَالمُشْتَرِطُ لِذَلِكَ هُوَ السَّرَّاجُ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ قَوْلِ ابْنِ عَرَفَةَ : وَيُقَدَّمُ الأَعَمُّ لِكَوْنِهِ أَعْرَفَ، وَفِي كَوْنِهِ وَاجِباً أَوْ أَوْلَى قَوْلَا السَّرَّاجِ وَالأَثِيرِ مَعَ الخُونَجِيِّ. اهـ .
The Bannani commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq: 'That it is the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body, or summarily as its definition by: 'that it is the rational animal'. In summary, the perfect definition is that which includes all essential attributes by direct reference or by implication, and no attention is paid to concomitant indication. It is called 'hadd' because hadd linguistically means 'prevention', and it prevents what is not among the individuals of the defined from entering into it, and prevents what is among the individuals of the defined from leaving it. It is called 'perfect' because it includes all the essential attributes completely. Marginal comment: His saying: 'sensing, voluntarily moving' – he combined both in defining 'animal', although one of them is sufficient to exclude what is not animal from among the types of growing things, as a reminder of the composition of the essence of 'animal' from both, in addition to corporeality and growth, so that it is distinguished from what shares with it in what precedes by these two powers, namely the perceiving and moving powers, even if one of them suffices to exclude what is not animal from among the types of growing things. For the conditions of definitions are intended to elucidate the essence, and exclusion is subsidiary, so its non-occurrence does not harm. His saying: 'by direct reference or by implication' – direct reference is mentioning the parts of the genus in detail, like defining 'human' as 'the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body', and implication is mentioning the parts summarily, like defining it as 'rational animal'. His saying: 'and no attention is paid to concomitant indication' means, like defining 'human' as 'rational', so it is not called a perfect definition. By his saying: 'because hadd linguistically means prevention' – from it is 'iron' (hadid), named so because it prevents enemies; and it is said: a woman observes 'iddah' (haddat) after her husband's death if she prevents herself from adornment; and the limits of Allah are what He has forbidden, or what prevents from violating them and transgressing to others; Allah says: 'These are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them' (2:187). His saying: 'and it is called perfect' – its apparent meaning is that order is not necessary for the perfection of the definition, i.e., that one should precede the genus before the differentia or proper accident when intending the perfect definition and perfect description. In the commentary on al-Mukhtasar by al-Sanusi: 'Some of them have stipulated order in its perfection by mentioning the more general part before the more specific part; if this order is reversed, it is not called a perfect definition by them, but rather an imperfect one.' The one who stipulated that is al-Sarraj, as can be inferred from Ibn 'Arafa's statement: 'The more general is presented because it is more known, and whether it is obligatory or preferable are two opinions from al-Sarraj and al-Athir along with al-Khunaji.'
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the perfect definition (al-hadd al-tamm). It begins by giving an example of a perfect definition: 'human' defined in full as 'the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving, rational body', or summarized as 'rational animal'. The key point is that a perfect definition includes all essential attributes of the definiendum, either by direct reference (mutabaqa) where the parts of the genus are listed in detail, or by implication (tadammun) where they are mentioned summarily. Concomitant indication (iltizam) is not sufficient; a definition by a single property like 'rational' for human is not perfect. The passage then explains the etymology of 'hadd' as 'prevention', illustrating with linguistic examples: iron (hadid) prevents enemies, a woman in 'iddah prevents herself from adornment, and the limits of Allah prevent transgression. This etymology reflects the logical function of a definition: it prevents non-members from entering and members from leaving. The reason for calling it 'perfect' (tamm) is its complete inclusion of essential attributes. A marginal note clarifies why both 'sensing' and 'voluntarily moving' are included in the definition of animal, even though one suffices for exclusion: the purpose is to exposit the composite essence of animal, not merely to exclude. Exclusion is subsidiary. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether ordering (tarteeb) – presenting the genus before the differentia or property – is required for a perfect definition. The apparent meaning of the text is that it is not required, but the commentator notes that some scholars like al-Sarraj stipulated it; their opinion is cited from al-Sanusi's commentary.

Key Terms
تَرْتِيب
حَدّ تامّ
حَدّ ناقص

A) It must include all essential attributes
B) It must have order (genus before differentia)

Key Takeaway
The necessity of order in definitions is a point of contention among logicians, with some requiring it and others considering it unnecessary.

The Complete Definition: Including All Essential Attributes
Author defines the complete definition and explains its components, etymology, and order condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a complete definition (hadd tamm) and an incomplete one by checking whether all essential attributes are included, either explicitly (mutabaqa) or implicitly (tadamun).

Core Idea
A complete definition must capture every essential attribute of the defined.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational concept of the complete definition. He gives two examples—one detailed and one summary—to illustrate how all essential attributes (dhatiyyat) can be included. Understanding this distinction is key to evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum in the passage. It is 'insan' (human) in the examples.
The passage uses 'insan' but the principle applies to any defined term.
2
Extract the essential attributes (dhatiyyat)
List the attributes that constitute the essence: in the detailed definition: body, growing, sensing, moving by will, rational. In the summary: animal and rational.
These are all dhatiyyat – parts of the quiddity.
3
Determine whether the definition uses correspondence (mutabaqa) or containment (tadamun)
If the parts are mentioned in detail, it's mutabaqa; if summarily (e.g., 'rational animal'), it's tadamun. Both are complete.
The passage explicitly defines these two modes.
4
Check for connotation (iltizam)
If a definition uses only one attribute like 'rational', it is incomplete because it relies on connotation, which is not sufficient.
The text says 'no consideration is given to connotation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ النَّاطِقُ ، أَوْ مُجْمَلَةٌ [ب/ ٢٦] ب: كَتَعْرِيفِهِ (۱) بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ». وَبِالجُمْلَةِ الحَدُّ النَّامُ هُوَ المُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزام. وَسُمِّيَ : «حَدًا»؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ»، وَهُوَ يَمْنَعُ غَيْرَ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ : فِيهِ، وَيَمْنَعُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الخُرُوج عَنْهُ . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا»؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِكَمَالِهَا . قصَّارة قوله : (الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ إِنَّما جمع بينهما في تعريف الحَيَوَان»، وإن كان أحدهما كاف في إخراج غير الحَيَوَان) مِن أنواع النَّامي ؛ تنبيهاً على تركب ماهيَّة «الحَيَوَان» منهما، زيادة على الجسميَّة والتُّموّ، فيمتاز عن مشاركه فيما قبل بهاتين القوتين وهما المدركة والمتحركة، وإن كانت إحداهما كافيةً في إخراج غير «الحَيَوَان مِن أنواع النَّامي، فقيود الحدود يُراد منها بيان الماهية، والإخراجُ تابع فلا يضرُّ تخلفه قوله : (بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ) المطابقةُ : ذكر أجزاء الجنس مفصَّلة كتعريف «الإنسان» بـ: «أنَّه : الجسم النامي ... إلخ»، والتّضمُّنُ : ذكر الأجزاء مجملةً كتعريفه بـ : الحَيَوَان النَّاطق». قوله : (وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزامِ :أي: كتعريف الإنسان» بـ «الناطق»، فلا يسمى : حدا تاما . بـ: قوله : (لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ») منه : الحَدِيدُ ؛ سمّي بذلك ؛ لأنَّه يمنعُ مِنَ الأعداء، ويُقالُ: حَدَّتِ المرأة على زوجها وأحدت إذا منعت نفسها مِنَ الزِّينة وحدود الله تعالى : ما منع منها، أو منع من مخالفتها والتَّعدي إلى غيرها ؛ قال الله تعالى : وتِلْكَ حُدُودُ اللَّهِ فَلَا تَقْرَبُوهَا ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۷] . قوله : (وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا» ظاهرُهُ : أَنَّه لا يُشترط في تمام الحد وجوب الترتيب، بأن يقدم الجنس عند إرادة الحدّ والرَّسم التَّامَّين على المميّز فصلاً أو خاصَّةً، وفي شرح المختصر للسنوسي : ومنهم من شرط في تمامه الترتيب بذكر الجزء الأعم مقدَّماً على ذكر الجزء الأخص، فإن عكس هذا الترتيب لم يسمَّ عند هؤلاء حدا تاما، بل ناقصاً . اهـ . " وَالمُشترط لذلك هو السَّرَّاج، كما يُؤخذ من قول ابن عرفة : ويقدم الأعم لكونه أعرف، وفي كونه واجباً أو أولى قولا السَّرَّاج والأثير مع الخونجي. اهـ . (۱) في (أ) : «كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ بدلاً من «كَتَعْرِيفِهِ».
That it is the body that grows, senses, moves by will, and speaks, or a summary [folio 26b]: like its definition by 'that it is the rational animal'. And in summary, the complete definition is that which includes all essential attributes by correspondence or by containment, and no consideration is given to the connotation. And it is called 'hadd' because hadd in language means 'prevention', and it prevents non-instances of the defined from entering into it, and prevents what are instances of the defined from leaving it. And it is called 'tamm' (complete) because of its inclusion of all essential attributes. Comment: His statement: 'the sensing, moving by will' – he only combined them in defining 'animal', even if one of them is sufficient to exclude non-animal from the types of growing things, to indicate the composition of the reality of 'animal' from them, in addition to corporeality and growth, so that it is distinguished from its shares in what precedes by these two powers, namely the perceptive and the motive, even if one of them is sufficient to exclude non-animal from the types of growing things. For the constraints of definitions are intended for exposition of the quiddity, and exclusion is subsidiary, so its failure does not harm. His statement: 'by correspondence or by containment' – correspondence is mentioning the parts of the genus in detail, like defining 'man' as 'that he is the growing body... etc.', and containment is mentioning the parts summarily, like defining him as 'the rational animal'. His statement: 'and no consideration is given to the connotation' – i.e., like defining 'man' by 'rational', so it is not called a complete definition. By his statement: 'because hadd in language is prevention' – from it is 'iron'; it is so named because it prevents from enemies. And it is said: the woman observed 'idda (haddat) over her husband and 'ahaddat' if she prevented herself from adornment. And the limits (hudud) of Allah are what He has prevented from, or what prevents from violating them and transgressing to others. Allah said: 'And those are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them' [Al-Baqarah 187]. His statement: 'and it is called tamm' – its apparent meaning is that in the completeness of the definition the necessity of order is not required, that is, that the genus be presented when intending the complete definition and description before the distinguishing differentia or property. And in the commentary of al-Mukhtasar by al-Sanusi: 'And among them is one who required in its completeness the order, by mentioning the more general part before the more specific part. If this order is reversed, it is not called a complete definition according to these, but an incomplete one.' End. The one who stipulated that is al-Sarraj, as is taken from the saying of Ibn 'Arafa: 'And the more general is presented because it is more known, and regarding whether it is obligatory or preferable, there are two opinions of al-Sarraj and al-Athir along with al-Khunaji.' End. (1) In (A): 'like defining man' instead of 'like its definition'.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage defines the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) and explains its components, etymology, and a disputed condition. The author begins by giving two examples: a detailed definition of man as 'the growing, sensing, moving by will, rational body' and a summary definition as 'the rational animal'. He then states that the complete definition includes all essential attributes (dhatiyyat), either explicitly through correspondence (mutabaqa) or implicitly through containment (tadamun). Connotation (iltizam) is not considered. The term 'hadd' is explained linguistically as 'prevention' – it prevents non-members from entering and members from leaving, illustrated by Quranic usage. It is called 'tamm' because it includes all essential attributes. The commentary then explains why both 'sensing' and 'moving by will' are mentioned together in the definition of 'animal' even though one suffices for exclusion – the purpose is to display the composite nature of the animal's reality. The commentator clarifies that constraints of definitions primarily aim to elucidate the quiddity, and exclusion is secondary. He then distinguishes between correspondence (explicit listing of parts) and containment (implicit summary). Connotation is excluded because it does not convey the essence directly; defining man by 'rational' alone is incomplete. The etymology is expanded with examples: iron (hadeed) prevents enemies, a woman in 'idda prevents herself from adornment, and Allah's limits prevent transgression (Quran 2:187). Finally, the author discusses whether order in definition is required – the apparent meaning suggests it is not, but some scholars (like al-Sarraj) require that the more general part be mentioned first, otherwise the definition is considered incomplete. This is presented as a disputed issue.

Key Takeaway
A complete definition must include all essential attributes, either explicitly or implicitly, and connotation alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a complete definition (hadd tamm) for the term 'salat' (prayer) in the Islamic legal sense, using either mutabaqa or tadamun. List all essential attributes (e.g., intention, takbir, recitation, bowing, prostration) and state whether your definition uses correspondence or containment.
?

The Function of Definition: Prevention and Precision
Author defines the complete definition and explains its components, etymology, and order condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a definition is called 'hadd' and how it prevents non-instances from entering and instances from leaving, using the Quranic example provided.

Core Idea
A definition both excludes what does not belong and includes what does.

The author delves into the etymology of 'hadd', revealing that its linguistic meaning of 'prevention' informs its logical function. He uses the Quranic verse to illustrate that a proper definition acts as a barrier. This lesson unpacks that function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root of 'hadd'
The author states that 'hadd' in language means 'prevention' (al-man'). This is the basis for its technical meaning.
2
Note the two prevention functions
The definition prevents non-members from entering and members from leaving. This means it must be both exclusive and exhaustive.
This is a key property of a good definition.
3
Examine the Quranic illustration
The author quotes Quran 2:187 'those are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them' to show that limits prevent transgression.
This reinforces the idea of boundaries.
4
Understand why connotation fails this function
Connotation (iltizam) does not provide a clear boundary because it does not directly include the genus; thus it cannot prevent non-instances from appearing to belong.
The text explicitly excludes it.
