«أَنَّهُ الجِسْمُ النَّامِي الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ النَّاطِقُ ، أَوْ مُجْمَلَةٌ [ب/ ٢٦] ب: كَتَعْرِيفِهِ (۱) بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ». وَبِالجُمْلَةِ الحَدُّ النَّامُ هُوَ المُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ، وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزام. وَسُمِّيَ : «حَدًا»؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ»، وَهُوَ يَمْنَعُ غَيْرَ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الدُّخُولِ : فِيهِ، وَيَمْنَعُ مَا هُوَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ مِنَ الخُرُوج عَنْهُ . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا»؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الذَّاتِيَّاتِ بِكَمَالِهَا . قصَّارة قوله : (الحَسَّاسُ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِالإِرَادَةِ إِنَّما جمع بينهما في تعريف الحَيَوَان»، وإن كان أحدهما كاف في إخراج غير الحَيَوَان) مِن أنواع النَّامي ؛ تنبيهاً على تركب ماهيَّة «الحَيَوَان» منهما، زيادة على الجسميَّة والتُّموّ، فيمتاز عن مشاركه فيما قبل بهاتين القوتين وهما المدركة والمتحركة، وإن كانت إحداهما كافيةً في إخراج غير «الحَيَوَان مِن أنواع النَّامي، فقيود الحدود يُراد منها بيان الماهية، والإخراجُ تابع فلا يضرُّ تخلفه قوله : (بِالمُطَابَقَةِ أَوْ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ) المطابقةُ : ذكر أجزاء الجنس مفصَّلة كتعريف «الإنسان» بـ: «أنَّه : الجسم النامي ... إلخ»، والتّضمُّنُ : ذكر الأجزاء مجملةً كتعريفه بـ : الحَيَوَان النَّاطق». قوله : (وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِدَلَالَةِ الاِلْتِزامِ :أي: كتعريف الإنسان» بـ «الناطق»، فلا يسمى : حدا تاما . بـ: قوله : (لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ لُغَةٌ : «المَنْعُ») منه : الحَدِيدُ ؛ سمّي بذلك ؛ لأنَّه يمنعُ مِنَ الأعداء، ويُقالُ: حَدَّتِ المرأة على زوجها وأحدت إذا منعت نفسها مِنَ الزِّينة وحدود الله تعالى : ما منع منها، أو منع من مخالفتها والتَّعدي إلى غيرها ؛ قال الله تعالى : وتِلْكَ حُدُودُ اللَّهِ فَلَا تَقْرَبُوهَا ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۷] . قوله : (وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا» ظاهرُهُ : أَنَّه لا يُشترط في تمام الحد وجوب الترتيب، بأن يقدم الجنس عند إرادة الحدّ والرَّسم التَّامَّين على المميّز فصلاً أو خاصَّةً، وفي شرح المختصر للسنوسي : ومنهم من شرط في تمامه الترتيب بذكر الجزء الأعم مقدَّماً على ذكر الجزء الأخص، فإن عكس هذا الترتيب لم يسمَّ عند هؤلاء حدا تاما، بل ناقصاً . اهـ . " وَالمُشترط لذلك هو السَّرَّاج، كما يُؤخذ من قول ابن عرفة : ويقدم الأعم لكونه أعرف، وفي كونه واجباً أو أولى قولا السَّرَّاج والأثير مع الخونجي. اهـ . (۱) في (أ) : «كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ بدلاً من «كَتَعْرِيفِهِ».
That it is the body that grows, senses, moves by will, and speaks, or a summary [folio 26b]: like its definition by 'that it is the rational animal'. And in summary, the complete definition is that which includes all essential attributes by correspondence or by containment, and no consideration is given to the connotation. And it is called 'hadd' because hadd in language means 'prevention', and it prevents non-instances of the defined from entering into it, and prevents what are instances of the defined from leaving it. And it is called 'tamm' (complete) because of its inclusion of all essential attributes. Comment: His statement: 'the sensing, moving by will' – he only combined them in defining 'animal', even if one of them is sufficient to exclude non-animal from the types of growing things, to indicate the composition of the reality of 'animal' from them, in addition to corporeality and growth, so that it is distinguished from its shares in what precedes by these two powers, namely the perceptive and the motive, even if one of them is sufficient to exclude non-animal from the types of growing things. For the constraints of definitions are intended for exposition of the quiddity, and exclusion is subsidiary, so its failure does not harm. His statement: 'by correspondence or by containment' – correspondence is mentioning the parts of the genus in detail, like defining 'man' as 'that he is the growing body... etc.', and containment is mentioning the parts summarily, like defining him as 'the rational animal'. His statement: 'and no consideration is given to the connotation' – i.e., like defining 'man' by 'rational', so it is not called a complete definition. By his statement: 'because hadd in language is prevention' – from it is 'iron'; it is so named because it prevents from enemies. And it is said: the woman observed 'idda (haddat) over her husband and 'ahaddat' if she prevented herself from adornment. And the limits (hudud) of Allah are what He has prevented from, or what prevents from violating them and transgressing to others. Allah said: 'And those are the limits of Allah, so do not approach them' [Al-Baqarah 187]. His statement: 'and it is called tamm' – its apparent meaning is that in the completeness of the definition the necessity of order is not required, that is, that the genus be presented when intending the complete definition and description before the distinguishing differentia or property. And in the commentary of al-Mukhtasar by al-Sanusi: 'And among them is one who required in its completeness the order, by mentioning the more general part before the more specific part. If this order is reversed, it is not called a complete definition according to these, but an incomplete one.' End. The one who stipulated that is al-Sarraj, as is taken from the saying of Ibn 'Arafa: 'And the more general is presented because it is more known, and regarding whether it is obligatory or preferable, there are two opinions of al-Sarraj and al-Athir along with al-Khunaji.' End. (1) In (A): 'like defining man' instead of 'like its definition'.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage defines the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) and explains its components, etymology, and a disputed condition. The author begins by giving two examples: a detailed definition of man as 'the growing, sensing, moving by will, rational body' and a summary definition as 'the rational animal'. He then states that the complete definition includes all essential attributes (dhatiyyat), either explicitly through correspondence (mutabaqa) or implicitly through containment (tadamun). Connotation (iltizam) is not considered. The term 'hadd' is explained linguistically as 'prevention' – it prevents non-members from entering and members from leaving, illustrated by Quranic usage. It is called 'tamm' because it includes all essential attributes. The commentary then explains why both 'sensing' and 'moving by will' are mentioned together in the definition of 'animal' even though one suffices for exclusion – the purpose is to display the composite nature of the animal's reality. The commentator clarifies that constraints of definitions primarily aim to elucidate the quiddity, and exclusion is secondary. He then distinguishes between correspondence (explicit listing of parts) and containment (implicit summary). Connotation is excluded because it does not convey the essence directly; defining man by 'rational' alone is incomplete. The etymology is expanded with examples: iron (hadeed) prevents enemies, a woman in 'idda prevents herself from adornment, and Allah's limits prevent transgression (Quran 2:187). Finally, the author discusses whether order in definition is required – the apparent meaning suggests it is not, but some scholars (like al-Sarraj) require that the more general part be mentioned first, otherwise the definition is considered incomplete. This is presented as a disputed issue.

Key Terms
حَد
المَنْع
حُدُودُ اللَّهِ

Why is a definition called 'hadd' in Arabic according to the passage?
A) Because it is sharp like iron
B) Because it prevents what is not the defined from entering and what is the defined from leaving
C) Because it is a limit set by Allah
D) Because it is derived from the word for 'new'

Key Takeaway
A definition serves as a boundary that precisely includes only what is defined and excludes everything else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the term 'kufr' (disbelief) and write a definition that prevents a Muslim from being incorrectly classified as a kafir and ensures that an actual kafir is not excluded. Check that your definition is both exclusive and exhaustive.
?
Using the concept of 'hadd' as prevention, critique the definition 'Iman is belief in the heart' – does it prevent non-Muslims from being considered mu'min? What is missing?

The Order of Genus and Differentia: A Scholarly Dispute
Author defines the complete definition and explains its components, etymology, and order condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the debate about whether stating the genus before the differentia is required for a complete definition, and identify the positions of al-Sarraj and al-Athir.

Core Idea
Some scholars require order in definitions; others hold that completeness does not depend on order.

The passage concludes with a subtle point: while the author initially suggests that order is not required, he cites a dispute. Scholars like al-Sarraj insist that the genus must come first. This lesson examines that debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent position of the author
The author says 'its apparent meaning is that in the completeness of the definition the necessity of order is not required'. This suggests a default view.
2
Find the dissenting view
He then quotes al-Sanusi's commentary: 'some require order' – mentioning the more general before the more specific.
3
Trace the key figures
The author names al-Sarraj as the one who stipulated order, and cites Ibn 'Arafa who presents two opinions: al-Sarraj and al-Athir with al-Khunaji.
This shows the debate within the tradition.
4
Consider the implications
If order is required, then reversing the genus and differentia (e.g., 'rational animal' instead of 'animal rational') would be incomplete. This affects how definitions are evaluated.
The passage leaves it as a disputed issue.

Commentary
This passage defines the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) and explains its components, etymology, and a disputed condition. The author begins by giving two examples: a detailed definition of man as 'the growing, sensing, moving by will, rational body' and a summary definition as 'the rational animal'. Constructing a Complete Description
Author defines complete description as genus and property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid complete description (rasm tamm) for any given term by selecting the proximate genus and a property that is both inclusive and necessary.

Core Idea
A complete description uses a property, not essence, to define.

The complete description (al-rasm al-tamm) is a key tool for defining terms when the essence is unknown. It combines the proximate genus with a distinguishing property that must cover all instances and always accompany the defined. This lesson unpacks the conditions of the property and shows how to apply them, using the classic example 'man is the laughing animal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term to be defined
Take a term from an Islamic science. For this lesson, we use 'insan' (human) as in the passage.
2
Find its proximate genus
Determine the immediate category to which the term belongs. For humans, it is 'animal' (hayawan).
The proximate genus is the nearest genus, not a distant one.
3
Select a property (khassa) that is unique to the term
Choose a distinguishing feature that belongs to all members of the term and only to them, but does not constitute their essence. The passage uses 'laughing' as a potentiality.
The property must be inclusive (shamila) and necessary (lazima).
4
Check inclusivity and necessity
Verify that every instance of the term possesses the property (inclusivity) and that the term cannot exist without it (necessity). For 'laughing potentially', all humans have this potential, and it is unique to humans. Actual laughter fails both conditions.
If a property fails either condition, it cannot be used in a complete description.
5
Assemble the complete description
Combine the proximate genus and the property: 'animal that laughs potentially' (al-hayawan al-dahik bil-quwwa).
This yields a complete description (rasm tamm), distinguishing the term from others.

وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الرَّسْمِ النَّامُ بِقَوْلِهِ : وَالرَّسْمُ بِـ : الجِنْسِ وَخَاصَةٍ مَعَا أَي : الرَّسْمُ التَّامُ هُوَ: «مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ وَالخَاصَّةِ، فَحَذَفَ أَيْضاً الوَصْفَ مِنَ الرَّسْمِ وَالجِنْسِ ؛ لِدَلَالَةِ مَا ذَكَرَهُ فِي الرَّسْمِ النَّاقِصِ عَلَيْهِ. وَالمُرَادُ : الخَاصَّةُ الشَّامِلَةُ اللَّازِمَةُ كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ الضَّاحِكُ» أَيْ: بِالقُوَّةِ، وَأَمَّا الضَّاحِكُ بِالفِعْلِ فَغَيْرُ شَامِلٍ وَلَا لَازِم [۲۸/۱]، فَلَا يُعَرَّفُ بِهِ؛ لِفَسَادِ عَكْسِهِ. وَسُمِّيَ : «رَسْماً» ؛ لِأَنَّ الرَّسْمَ فِي اللُّغَةِ [ج/ ۱۸]: «أَثَرُ الشَّيْء وَعَلَامَتُهُ، وَالخَاصَّةُ أَثَرٌ وَعَلَامَةٌ لِمَا اخْتُصَّ بِهَا . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا » ؛ لِذِكْرِ الجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ فِيهِ، فَفِيهِ كَمَال بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلنَّاقِصِ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ ذِكْرُ الفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ .
And he pointed to the complete description by his saying: 'And the description is by the genus and a property together', meaning: The complete description is 'what is composed of the proximate genus and the property'. So he also omitted the adjective from the description and the genus due to the indication of what he mentioned in the incomplete description upon it. And what is intended is the inclusive, necessary property, like the definition of 'man' by 'that he is the laughing animal', i.e., potentially. As for laughing actually, it is not inclusive nor necessary [folio 28a], so it is not used to define him, because its converse would be invalid. And it is called 'rasm' because rasm in language [folio 18] is 'the trace of a thing and its mark', and the property is a trace and a mark of that which is distinct by it. And it is called 'tamm' because of the mention of the proximate genus in it, so it has completeness relative to the incomplete one, and the difference between the differentia and the property has preceded.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage defines the complete description (al-rasm al-tamm) and distinguishes it from the complete definition. The author explains that the complete description is composed of the proximate genus and a property (khassa), as indicated by the verse. Unlike the complete definition, which uses the differentia (fasl), the complete description substitutes a distinguishing property. The property must be both inclusive (shamila) – covering all instances of the defined – and necessary (lazima) – always accompanying the defined. The example given is 'man is the laughing animal'. This means 'laughing' as a potentiality, not actual laughter, because actual laughter is not present in all humans at all times and is not necessary. If one used actual laughter, the definition would fail the condition of conversion (the converse 'every laugher is human' would be false). The term 'rasm' is derived from the linguistic meaning 'trace' or 'mark', as the property is a distinguishing mark of the defined. It is called 'tamm' (complete) because it includes the proximate genus, giving it completeness relative to the incomplete description which lacks the genus. The passage also notes that the difference between differentia and property has been previously explained.

Key Terms
الرَّسْمُ التَّام
الخَاصَّة

Key Takeaway
A complete description combines the proximate genus with a property that is both inclusive and necessary, distinguishing the defined without capturing its essence.

The Incomplete Definition: Al-Hadd al-Naqis
Author defines the incomplete definition as using only the differentia or the differentia with a distant genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete definition (hadd naqis) and distinguish it from a complete definition by checking for the absence of the proximate genus.

Core Idea
An incomplete definition omits the proximate genus, using only the differentia or a distant genus.

In the previous lesson, you learned the requirements for a complete definition (hadd tamm). Now we turn to its counterpart: the incomplete definition (hadd naqis). The passage clarifies that an incomplete definition does not include the proximate genus. It may consist of the differentia alone or the differentia with a distant genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the defined term (al-ma‘rūf)
The passage uses 'man' (al-insān) as its example. Identify the term being defined – this is the species whose definition is under discussion.
2
Examine the defining components
List the components given: for the first type, only 'rational' (al-nāṭiq); for the second type, 'rational body' (al-jism al-nāṭiq). Notice that the proximate genus 'animal' (al-ḥayawān) is missing in both.
3
Determine the type of incompleteness
Ask: Is the proximate genus absent? If yes, it is a ḥadd nāqis. Then classify: it is either by differentia alone (fasl faqat) or by differentia with a distant genus (fasl ma‘a jins ba‘īd). In the passage, the first is 'rational', the second is 'rational body'.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Consider defining 'khamr' (wine) as 'al-muskir' (the intoxicant). Does it include the proximate genus 'sharāb' (beverage)? It does not, so it is incomplete. This shows how a missing genus can lead to misapplication of rulings.
5
Verify using the conditions from the passage
Al-Sanusi and al-Hilali note that the purpose of definition (tamyīz or taṣawwur) is still attained even without order. Check whether the incomplete definition still fulfills its basic function – distinguishing the species or providing conceptualization.

وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الحَدِّ النَّاقِصِ بِقَوْلِهِ : (٤٢) وَنَاقِصُ الحَدٌ بـ : فَصْلٍ، أَوْ مَعَا جِنْسِ بَعيدٍ، لَا قَرِيبٍ وَقَعَـا بـ: يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الحَدَّ النَّاقِصَ لَا يُذْكَرُ فِيهِ الجِنْسُ القَرِيبُ، بَلْ يَكُونُ إِمَّا بِالفَصْلِ وَحْدَهُ كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ : «النَّاطِقِ ، أَوْ بِالفَصْلِ مَعَ الجِنْسِ البَعِيدِ كَتَعْرِيفِهِ بِـ : الجِسْمِ النَّاطِقِ» . قال السَّنوسي في «شرحه» للخونجي : وهذا الثَّاني أظهر؛ لأنَّ المقصود مِنَ التَّعريف، وهو التَّمييز أو تصور المعرَّف بذاتياته، أو ببعضها لا يختل بسبب عدم ملاحظة الترتيب. وقال الهلالي : ولا يشكَّ أنَّ الغرض مِنَ التّامّ حاصل مع الترتيب ودونه . قول النَّاظم : (وَنَاقِصُ الحَد بـ: فَصْلِ) فيه إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف؛ أي: والحد النَّاقص. وقوله : (بِفَضل) أي قريب وهو مبني على جواز التّعريف بالمفرد، وفيه خلاف .
And he pointed to the incomplete definition by his saying: (42) 'And the incomplete of the definition is by a differentia, or with a distant genus, not a proximate, it occurs.' Meaning: That the incomplete definition does not mention the proximate genus, but rather it is either by the differentia alone, like the definition of 'man' by 'the rational', or by the differentia with the distant genus, like his definition by 'the rational body'. Al-Sanusi said in his commentary on al-Khunaji: 'And this second type is more apparent, because the purpose of definition, which is distinction or conceptualization of the defined by its essential attributes or some of them, is not impaired by the lack of observing order.' And al-Hilali said: 'There is no doubt that the purpose of the complete [definition] is attained with order and without it.' The statement of the versifier: 'and incomplete of the definition by a differentia' – in it there is addition of the adjective to the described; i.e., 'and the incomplete definition'. And his statement: 'by a differentia' meaning proximate, and it is based on the permissibility of definition by a single term, and there is disagreement about it.

Commentary
This passage defines the incomplete definition (al-hadd al-naqis) as one that does not include the proximate genus. The author, through the verse, states that it is either by the differentia alone (e.g., defining 'man' as 'the rational') or by the differentia with a distant genus (e.g., 'the rational body'). The commentary clarifies that the proximate genus is absent. Al-Sanusi and al-Hilali are quoted to support that order is not required for completion – the purpose of definition (distinction or conceptualization) is achieved even without order. The verse's wording is also analyzed grammatically: 'incomplete of the definition' is an idafa where the adjective is added to the described, meaning 'the incomplete definition'. The phrase 'by a differentia' refers to a proximate differentia (the immediate differentia of the species). The commentator notes that using a single term for definition (e.g., just 'rational') is a point of dispute, implying that some scholars allow it while others do not.

Definition of Complete Rasm (الرسم التام)
Author defines complete rasm as composed of near genus and necessary property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a complete rasm and verify whether a given definition meets the conditions of comprehensiveness and necessity.

Core Idea
A complete rasm uses the near genus and a comprehensive necessary property to define a species.

In classical Islamic logic, definitions are categorized into hadd (essential) and rasm (accidental). The complete rasm is a type of definition that, while not using the essential differentia, still achieves perfect distinction by combining the near genus with a property that is both comprehensive and necessary. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary explains the structure and conditions of a complete rasm, using the classic example of 'man' as 'the rational animal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of complete rasm
Find the two necessary parts: the near genus (al-jins al-qarīb) and a property (khāṣṣa). In the passage, the complete rasm is said to be composed of these two.
The property must be accidental, not essential like the differentia.
2
Understand the conditions for the property
The property must be comprehensive (shāmila) – applying to every individual of the species – and necessary (lāzima) – constant and inseparable from the essence. The passage stresses these conditions.
The author uses 'comprehensive necessary property' explicitly.
3
Analyze the example: 'man' as 'rational animal'
The genus is 'animal' (near genus). The property 'rational' (potentially) is comprehensive (all humans have the potential) and necessary (it is a defining characteristic). This yields a valid complete rasm.
The property must be understood as 'bi-l-quwwa' (potentially), not 'bi-l-fi'l' (actually), as actual laugher is not comprehensive.
4
Contrast with inadequate definitions
Consider 'man' as 'actual laugher' – not comprehensive because not always laughing; or 'actual breather' – not necessary because breathing can stop. These fail the conditions and corrupt the definition (فساد عكسه).
The condition of convertibility (in‘ikās) ensures the definition matches all and only the definiendum.
5
Recognize the rationale for the name
'Rasm' means trace or sign in language, referring to the property as a distinguishing sign. 'Complete' (tāmm) indicates inclusion of the near genus, giving perfection over incomplete rasm.
The passage also notes that order of components does not affect the purpose of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرفات وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الرَّسْمِ النَّامِ بِقَوْلِهِ : وَالرَّسْمُ بِـ : الجِنْسِ وَخَاصَةٍ مَعَا أَي : الرَّسْمُ التَّامُ هُوَ: «مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ وَالخَاصَّةِ، فَحَذَفَ أَيْضاً الوَصْفَ مِنَ الرَّسْمِ وَالجِنْسِ ؛ لِدَلَالَةِ مَا ذَكَرَهُ فِي الرَّسْمِ النَّاقِصِ عَلَيْهِ. وَالمُرَادُ : الخَاصَّةُ الشَّامِلَةُ اللَّازِمَةُ كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ الحَيَوَانُ الضَّاحِكُ» أَيْ: بِالقُوَّةِ، وَأَمَّا الضَّاحِكُ بِالفِعْلِ فَغَيْرُ شَامِلٍ وَلَا لَازِم [۲۸/۱]، فَلَا يُعَرَّفُ بِهِ؛ لِفَسَادِ عَكْسِهِ. وَسُمِّيَ : «رَسْماً» ؛ لِأَنَّ الرَّسْمَ فِي اللُّغَةِ [ج/ ۱۸]: «أَثَرُ الشَّيْء وَعَلَامَتُهُ، وَالخَاصَّةُ أَثَرٌ وَعَلَامَةٌ لِمَا اخْتُصَّ بِهَا . وَسُمِّيَ : «تَامَّا » ؛ لِذِكْرِ الجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ فِيهِ، فَفِيهِ كَمَال بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلنَّاقِصِ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ ذِكْرُ الفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ . قال السَّنوسي في «شرحه» للخونجي : وهذا الثَّاني أظهر؛ لأنَّ المقصود مِنَ التَّعريف، وهو التَّمييز أو تصور المعرَّف بذاتياته، أو ببعضها لا يختل بسبب عدم ملاحظة الترتيب. وَقَالَ الْهِلَالِي : وَلَا يُشَكُّ أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ مِنَ التَّامِّ حَاصِلٌ مَعَ التَّرْتِيبِ وَدُونَهُ . قَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ : (وَخَاصَةٍ) فِي هَذَا الْبَيْتِ، وَالثَّالِثُ بَعْدَهُ بِتَخْفِيفِ الصَّادِ ضَرُورَةً؛ إِذْ لَا يَجُوزُ الْتِقَاءُ السَّاكِنَيْنِ فِي الشِّعْرِ إِلَّا ضَرُورَةً. قَوْلُهُ : (وَالمُرَادُ : الخَاصَّةُ الشَّامِلَةُ اللَّازِمَةُ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى أَنَّ مُرَادَهُ مَا ذَكَرَ : قَرِينَةُ شَرْطِ الِانْعِكَاسِ، وَاحْتَرَزَ بِـ «الشَّامِلَةِ» : مِنْ غَيْرِ الشَّامِلَةِ كَتَعْرِيفِ الْإِنْسَانِ» بِـ: «الضَّاحِكِ بِالْفِعْلِ»، وَبِـ «اللَّازِمَةِ» : مِنْ غَيْرِ اللَّازِمَةِ كَتَعْرِيفِ الْإِنْسَانِ» بِـ: «الْمُتَنَفِّسِ بِالْفِعْلِ.
Chapter on definitions. And he pointed to the complete rasm by his saying: 'And the rasm is by the genus and a property together.' Meaning: The complete rasm is 'that which is composed of the near genus and the property.' He omitted the description from the rasm and the genus due to the indication of what he mentioned in the incomplete rasm. And what is meant is the comprehensive necessary property, like the definition of 'man' as 'the rational animal' i.e., potentially. As for the actual laugher, it is neither comprehensive nor necessary, so it cannot be used for definition due to the corruption of its converse. And it is called 'rasm' because 'rasm' in language means 'trace and sign of a thing', and the property is a trace and sign for that which it distinguishes. And it is called 'complete' because the near genus is mentioned in it, so there is perfection relative to the incomplete. And the difference between the differentia and property has preceded. Al-Sanūsī in his commentary on al-Khūnajī said: 'This second is more apparent, because the purpose of definition, which is distinction or conceptualization of the definiendum by its essentials or some of them, is not impaired by not observing order.' The author's saying: '(and a property) in this line, and the third after it with a lightening of the ṣād for necessity, because the meeting of two quiescent letters in poetry is not permissible except for necessity.' His saying: '(and what is meant is the comprehensive necessary property) the evidence that what he intends is what he mentioned: the contextual indication of the condition of convertibility. And by 'comprehensive' he excludes what is not comprehensive, like the definition of 'man' as 'the actual laugher', and by 'necessary' excludes what is not necessary, like the definition of 'man' as 'the actual breather.'
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage defines the complete rasm (الرسم التام), which is one of the types of definitions in logic. The author begins by citing a line from the matn (the poem) that says 'the rasm is by the genus and a property together.' He then explains that the complete rasm is composed of the near genus and a property. The property referred to must be both comprehensive (shāmila) and necessary (lāzima). He gives the example of defining 'man' as 'the rational animal' (al-ḥayawān al-ḍāḥik), where 'rational' is a property that is comprehensive (applies to all humans) and necessary (only humans have it potentially). He points out that if we used 'actual laugher', it would not be comprehensive because not all humans are actually laughing at a given moment, and it would not be necessary because it is not a constant attribute. Therefore, such a definition would fail the condition of convertibility (in‘ikās). The author then explains the terminology: 'rasm' in language means a trace or sign, and the property is a trace of what it distinguishes. 'Complete' (tāmm) because it includes the near genus, which gives it perfection relative to the incomplete rasm. The author also notes that the difference between the differentia (faṣl) and property (khāṣṣa) has been explained earlier. He then quotes Al-Sanūsī and Al-Hilālī to address a potential issue: whether the order of components matters. They assert that the purpose of definition is achieved with or without order. Finally, a linguistic note is added about the word 'ķāṣṣa' in the poem, where the letter ṣād is lightened for poetic necessity.

Key Terms
رسم (rasm): A definition that uses accidental properties (ʿaraḍ) rather than essential differentia. [Note: Stable term for description (as opposed to hadd) across the tradition.]
خاصة (khāṣṣa): A property that is peculiar to a species, comprehensive and necessary, but not part of its essence. [Note: Distinguished from faṣl (differentia) which is essential; khāṣṣa is always accidental.]
جنس قريب (jins qarīb): The nearest genus that immediately contains the species. [Note: Stable term; used consistently in definitions.]
تام (tāmm): Complete in the sense that it includes the near genus, making it more perfect than incomplete rasm. [Note: Stable across logical texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A complete rasm is a definition using the near genus and a comprehensive necessary property, providing a perfect description without relying on the essential differentia.

Definition of Incomplete Hadd (الحد الناقص)
Author defines incomplete hadd as composed of a differentia alone or with a remote genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete hadd and explain why it is incomplete.

Core Idea
Incomplete hadd lacks the near genus, using only differentia or remote genus.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the complete hadd that includes both the near genus and the differentia. But what if a definition only gives the differentia, like calling man 'rational' without mentioning 'animal'? This is an incomplete hadd (ḥadd nāqiṣ). In this passage, the commentator explains the line from the poem and clarifies the concept, its nomenclature, and the scholarly disagreement over defining with a single term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'incomplete hadd' (الحد الناقص). Notice the construction: the author says 'And the incomplete hadd is by a differentia' – here 'hadd' is the described and 'nāqiṣ' is the adjective.
2
Extract the components of the definition
The incomplete hadd consists of either just the near differentia (faṣl qarīb) or the differentia together with a remote genus (jins ba‘īd). The near genus is absent.
The differentia is always essential.
3
Understand the controversy
The author notes that this type of definition relies on the permissibility of defining with a single word (ta‘rīf bi-l-mufrad). Some logicians permit it; others require a composite definition (genus + differentia).
This disagreement affects whether an incomplete hadd is accepted as a valid definition.
4
Analyze the naming rationale
It is called 'hadd' because it contains the essential differentia (the core of definition), and 'nāqiṣ' (incomplete) because it lacks some of the essential components – namely, the near genus.
The author emphasizes that the essential components are genus and differentia; missing one makes it incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول النَّاظم : (وَنَاقِصُ الحَد بـ: فَصْلِ) فيه إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف؛ أي: والحد النَّاقص. وقوله : (بِفَضل) أي قريب وهو مبني على جواز التّعريف بالمفرد، وفيه خلاف ; سُمِّيَ : «حَدَّا» ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وَنَاقِصاً؛ لِنَقْصِ بَعْضِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ مِنْهُ
The author's saying: '(And the incomplete hadd is by a differentia)' in it, the adjective is added to the described; i.e., the incomplete hadd. And his saying: '(by a differentia)' i.e., near, and it is based on the permissibility of definition by a single word, and there is disagreement about it. It is called 'hadd' for what has passed, and 'incomplete' due to the lack of some of the essentials from it.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage explains the incomplete hadd (الحد الناقص). The author quotes a line from the poem: 'And incomplete hadd is by a differentia.' He explains that the term 'hadd' here is the described (the incomplete hadd), and 'nāqiṣ' is the adjective. The differentia (faṣl) intended is the near differentia. He notes that this definition assumes that it is permissible to define with a single term (like just 'rational' for man), though there is scholarly disagreement on this point. The definition is called 'hadd' because it uses the essential differentia, and 'incomplete' because it lacks some of the essential components (like the genus). The essential components are the genus and differentia; when the genus is missing, the definition is incomplete.

D) Because it is not convertible.

Key Takeaway
An incomplete hadd is a valid definition in form but is less perfect because it lacks the near genus, relying solely on the differentia or a remote genus.

Incomplete Rasm: Definitions Using Property Alone or with Remote Genus
Author defines incomplete rasm as composed of a property alone or with a remote genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete rasm and distinguish its two forms: by property alone or by remote genus with property.

Core Idea
Incomplete rasm defines through a unique property, not the near genus.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the matn's definition of incomplete rasm. We will see how a definition can be valid yet incomplete when it uses only a property (either simple or composite) or a remote genus with a property. Understanding this helps us evaluate the precision of definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'rasm nāqiṣ' in the Arabic. It is the definiendum of this passage.
2
Extract the two forms of incomplete rasm
Note that the author presents two forms: (1) by property alone (simple or composite), and (2) by remote genus plus property. Write them down.
A composite property is a combination of general accidents that together are unique.
3
Analyze the examples
For property alone: 'laugher' for man (simple); 'upright stature, clear skin, broad nails' (composite). For remote genus + property: 'rational body'. Check why each component is general.
Each component of the composite property individually is a general accident, but the combination is unique.
4
Determine why it is incomplete
Compare with complete rasm, which includes the near genus and essential differentiae. Incomplete rasm lacks these, capturing only a unique property.
The commentator says: 'some or all of what the complete rasm contains of essentials is missing.'
5
Evaluate the role of the remote genus
In the second form, the remote genus (e.g., 'body' for man) is not the near genus ('animal'). The more remote the genus, the more deficient the definition.
The matn says 'abʿad' (more remote), and the commentator notes this increases deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الرَّسْمِ النَّاقِصِ بِقَوْلِهِ : (٤٣) وَنَاقِصُ الرَّسْمِ بِـ خَاصَةٍ فَقَط أَوْ مَعَ جِنْسِ أَبْعَدِ قَدِ ارْتَبَط يَعْنِي: أَنَّ الرَّسْمَ النَّاقِصَ هُوَ : «مَا يَكُونُ بِالخَاصَّةِ وَحْدَهَا»؛ مُفْرَدَةٌ كَانَتْ كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «الصَّاحِكِ»، وَالمُرَادُ : الصَّاحِكُ بِالقُوَّةِ، أَوْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ كَتَعْرِيفِهِ بِـ : «المُنْتَصِبِ القَامَةِ البَادِي البَشَرَةِ العَرِيضِ الأَظْفَارِ، فَمَجْمُوعُ هَذِهِ العَوَارِضِ الثَّلَاثَةِ خَاصَّةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهَا بِالْفِرَادِهِ عَرَضٌ عَامٌ ، أَوْ يَكُونُ بِالجِنْسِ البَعِيدِ وَالخَاصَّةِ كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ» بـ : الجِسْمِ الضَّاحِكِ . سُمِّيَ : رَسْماً» ؛ لِمَا سَبَقَ، وَنَاقِصاً ; لِأَنَّهُ نَقَصَ مِنْهُ بَعْضُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهِ الرَّسْمُ التَّامُ مِنَ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ أَوْ جَمِيعِهِ . قوله : (أَبْعَدِ) أي بعيد وصرفه ضرورةً، وكلَّما كان الجنس أبعد كان التعريف بالنقصان أَدْخَل . قوله : (وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهَا بِالْفِرَادِهِ عَرَضُ عَامٌ أي : فالمنتصب القامة يصدق بـ «الإنسان» وبـ «الطائر» ; إذ المراد به: من يمشي على رجلين البادي البشرة; أي: الجلد يصدق به وبـ «الحوت»، والعريض الأظفار يصدق به وبـ البقر والإبل مثلاً .
And he pointed to the incomplete rasm by his saying: (43) 'And incomplete rasm is by a property alone or with a more remote genus that is connected.' Meaning: The incomplete rasm is 'that which is by the property alone', whether it is single, like the definition of 'man' as 'the laugher' (and what is meant is the potential laugher), or composite, like his definition by 'the upright stature, the clear skin, the broad nails' — the sum of these three accidents is a single property, and each one individually is a general accident. Or it may be by the remote genus and the property, like the definition of 'man' as 'the rational body'. It is called 'rasm' for what preceded, and 'incomplete' because some or all of what the complete rasm contains of essentials is missing from it. His saying: '(more remote)' i.e., distant, and it is declined for poetic necessity, and the more remote the genus, the more deficient the definition. His saying: '(and each one of them individually is a general accident)' i.e., upright stature applies to 'man' and to 'bird', because what is meant is one who walks on two legs; clear skin (i.e., the skin) applies to it and to 'fish'; broad nails applies to it and to 'cattle' and 'camels' for example.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage defines the incomplete rasm (الرسم الناقص). The author cites matn line (43): 'And incomplete rasm is by a property alone or with a more remote genus that is connected.' He explains that the incomplete rasm is either by a single property (like 'laugher' for man, referring to the potential laugher) or by a composite property (like a combination of three accidents: upright stature, clear skin, broad nails). He notes that each of these three accidents individually is a general accident (ʿaraḍ ‘āmm) applying to other species, but together they form a unique property. Alternatively, the incomplete rasm can be by a remote genus and a property, like 'rational body'. The term 'rasm' is used for the same reason as before, and 'incomplete' because some or all of the essentials that the complete rasm contains are missing. He then comments on the word 'ab‘ad' (more remote) in the matn, explaining that it is declined for poetic necessity, and that the more remote the genus, the more deficient the definition. Finally, he elaborates on the example of the composite property, showing that each component is a general accident: upright stature applies to birds, clear skin applies to fish, broad nails apply to cattle and camels. Thus, only the combination distinguishes humans.

عرض عام

An incomplete rasm uses only a property (simple or composite) or a property with a remote genus, missing the near genus and essential differentiae.

Verbal Definition: Replacing a Word with a Known Synonym
Author defines verbal definition as word substitution by a synonym.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a verbal definition (taʿrīf lafẓī) in Islamic texts and distinguish it from a real definition (ḥadd or rasm).

Core Idea
Verbal definition replaces an obscure word with a familiar synonym — it clarifies language, not essence.

In this passage, the author defines the verbal definition (التعريف اللفظي), a basic tool in Islamic logic and lexicography. Unlike real definitions that analyze the essence of a thing, verbal definitions merely substitute an unknown term with a known one. This lesson will help you recognize when a definition is purely linguistic versus conceptual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'تعريف لفظي' in the Arabic. The passage describes it as the definer that is famous among them.
2
Extract the core operation
The definition says it is 'تبديل لفظ بردف أشهر' (replacement of a word with a more famous synonym). Understand that verbal definition is an operation of substitution, not analysis.
The word 'رديف' means synonym.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives 'الهزبر: الأسد' and 'العسجد: الذهب' as instances. Note that the original word is less known, and the substitute is more famous.
These are purely verbal clarifications.
4
Distinguish from real definition
Real definitions (ḥadd/rasm) provide genus and differentia, revealing the essence. Verbal definitions only provide a synonym. Check whether a given definition adds conceptual knowledge or just lexical equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا بِـ «لَفْظِيّ» لَدَيْهِمْ شُهِرَا تَبْدِيلُ لَفْظُ بِرَدِيفٍ أَشْهَرَا يَعْنِي: أَنَّ المُعَرِّفَ الَّذِي اشْتُهِرَ عِنْدَهُمْ تَسْمِيَتُهُ بـ «اللَّفْظِيّ» هُوَ: «الَّذِي يُقْصَدُ بِهِ تَفْسِيرُ اللَّفْظ» ; بِأَنْ لَا يَكُونَ اللَّفْظُ وَاضِحاً فِي الدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى مَعْنِّى ، فَيُبَدَّلُ بِلَفْظُ آخَرَ أَشْهَرَ مِنْهُ الدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى ذَلِكَ المَعْنَى ; كَأَنْ يُقَالَ: «الهِزْبَرُ : الأَسَدُ» ، وَ: «العَسْجَدُ : الذَّهَبُ». قوله : (الهِزْبَرُ) كـ «سَجَل، ودِرهم، ومن أسمائه أيضاً : الهرماس والغضنفر .
And what is famous among them as 'lafẓī' (verbal) is: the replacement of a word with a more famous synonym. Meaning: The definer that is famous among them to be termed 'lafẓī' is 'that by which the meaning of the word is intended to be explained'; i.e., when the word is not clear in indicating a meaning, it is replaced by another word that is more famous in indicating that meaning, like saying 'al-hizabr: the lion', and 'al-ʿasjad: gold'. His saying: '(al-hizabr)' like 'sajal' and 'dirham', and among its names also al-hirmās and al-ghaḍanfar.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage defines the verbal definition (التعريف اللفظي). The author quotes a line from the matn saying that it is 'the replacement of a word with a more famous synonym.' He explains that this type of definition is intended to clarify the meaning of an obscure word by substituting it with a more well-known word that has the same meaning. Examples include 'al-hizabr' meaning 'the lion' (al-asad), and 'al-ʿasjad' meaning 'gold'. The author adds that 'al-hizabr' is like 'sajal' and 'dirham' (which are also synonyms for lion), and other names for lion include 'al-hirmās' and 'al-ghaḍanfar'. This type of definition is purely linguistic and does not add conceptual knowledge; it merely clarifies the term.

مبدل
رديف

Incomplete Definition by Accidental Properties (al-Rasm al-Naqis)
Author explains the incomplete definition by property alone or with distant genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete definition (rasm naqis) by its structure: either by a single property, a compound property, or a distant genus with a property, and distinguish it from a complete definition.

Core Idea
An incomplete definition uses accidental properties, missing essential attributes.

In this passage, the author explains what makes a definition incomplete (rasm naqis). Unlike the complete definition (hadd) which uses the essence (fasl) and proximate genus, the incomplete definition relies on accidents (khassa) and may include a distant genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial in Islamic logic for evaluating definitions in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property (khassa) used
Look for an accidental property that is coextensive with the defined term. In the example for 'human', the property is 'laughing' (a capacity).
2
Check if the genus is proximate or distant
The author says if the genus is other than proximate (i.e., distant), the rasm is incomplete. For 'human', using 'body' (jism) instead of 'animal' (hayawan) makes it distant.
3
Examine whether the property is single or compound
The property can be a single accident ('laughing') or a combination of accidents that together form one khassa (e.g., upright stature, fair skin, broad nails for human).
Each individual accident in the compound is a common accident, but their conjunction forms a unique property.
4
Determine completeness
If the definition uses only a property (with or without a distant genus) and lacks the proximate genus and essential differentia, it is incomplete (rasm naqis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنَاقِصُ الرَّسْمِ بِـ خَاصَةٍ فَقَط أَوْ مَعَ جِنْسِ أَبْعَدِ قَدِ ارْتَبَط يَعْنِي: أَنَّ الرَّسْمَ النَّاقِصَ هُوَ : «مَا يَكُونُ بِالخَاصَّةِ وَحْدَهَا»؛ مُفْرَدَةٌ كَانَتْ كَتَعْرِيفِ «الإِنْسَانِ» بـ: «الصَّاحِكِ»، وَالمُرَادُ : الصَّاحِكُ بِالقُوَّةِ، أَوْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ كَتَعْرِيفِهِ بِـ : «المُنْتَصِبِ القَامَةِ البَادِي البَشَرَةِ العَرِيضِ الأَظْفَارِ، فَمَجْمُوعُ هَذِهِ العَوَارِضِ الثَّلَاثَةِ خَاصَّةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهَا بِالْفِرَادِهِ عَرَضٌ عَامٌ ، أَوْ يَكُونُ بِالجِنْسِ البَعِيدِ وَالخَاصَّةِ كَتَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ» بـ : الجِسْمِ الضَّاحِكِ . سُمِّيَ : رَسْماً» ؛ لِمَا سَبَقَ، وَنَاقِصاً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَقَصَ مِنْهُ بَعْضُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهِ الرَّسْمُ التَّامُ مِنَ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ أَوْ جَمِيعِهِ . وَحَاصِلُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ : أَنَّ التَّعْرِيف بِالفَصْلِ حَدٌ، وَبِالخَاصَّةِ رَسُمٌ ; ثُمَّ مَا كَانَ مِنْهُمَا مَعَ الجِنْسِ القَرِيبِ تَامّ، وَمَا كَانَ بِغَيْرِ القَرِيبِ نَاقِصُ .
And the incomplete composition (rasm) is by a property (khassa) alone, or with a distant genus it is connected. Meaning: that the incomplete composition is that which is by the property alone, whether singular, like the definition of 'human' by 'the laughing one', and what is meant is 'the one capable of laughter by potentiality', or compound, like his definition by 'the upright of stature, the fair of skin, the broad of nails', so the total of these three accidental properties is a single property, and each one of them individually is a common accident; or it is by the distant genus and the property, like the definition of 'human' by 'the laughing body'. It is called 'composition' (rasm) for what preceded, and 'incomplete' because some of what the complete composition includes of essential attributes is missing from it, or all of them. And the summary of what preceded is: that definition by the differential (fasl) is a definition by essence (hadd), and by the property (khassa) is a composition (rasm); then whatever of them is with the proximate genus is complete, and whatever is with other than the proximate is incomplete.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage defines the incomplete definition by property (al-rasm al-naqis). The author begins by quoting the matn line: 'And the incomplete composition is by a property alone or with a distant genus it is connected.' He then explains that the incomplete composition is one that uses only an accident (khassa) by itself, either singular (e.g., 'human' defined as 'the laughing one' in potentiality) or compound (e.g., a combination of three accidents that together form a single property: upright stature, fair complexion, broad nails; each alone is a common accident). Alternatively, it can be by a distant genus and a property, like 'human' defined as 'laughing body'. The term 'incomplete' is used because it lacks some or all of the essential attributes that the complete composition would include. Finally, the author summarizes: definition by fasl is a complete definition (hadd), by khassa is a rasm; if either includes the proximate genus, it is complete; otherwise, incomplete.

Key Terms
رسم ناقص
خَاصَّة
جِنْس بَعِيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) الإنسان بالناطق
B) الإنسان بالجسم الضاحك
C) الإنسان بالصاحك
D) الإنسان بالكاتب

Key Takeaway
An incomplete definition (rasm naqis) is built from accidental properties alone or with a distant genus, and it lacks the essential differentia and proximate genus of a complete definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Definition by Example: Similitude and Particular Instances
Author explains definition by example/similitude and its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition by similitude and definition by particular instance, and identify examples of each in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Definition by example clarifies by comparison or concrete case.

In this passage, the author explains definition by example (al-ta'rif bi'l-mithal). This method helps make an abstract concept understandable by likening it to something familiar or by giving a specific instance. As we read, pay attention to the two subtypes and how they function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes
The author mentions two forms: definition by similitude (tashbih) and definition by particular instance (dhikr juz'i). Locate each in the text.
2
Analyze the similitude example
The author compares knowledge to visual perception. What is the point of comparison? Both involve the imprinting of an image in a faculty.
3
Analyze the particular instance example
The author says knowledge is like our perception that one is half of two. How does this particular instance help define knowledge? It points to a concrete case of knowledge.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a new example, decide whether it is similitude or particular instance. For example, 'Piety is like a light that guides the heart.' This is similitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا التَّعْرِيفُ بِالمِثَالِ وَهُوَ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالشِّبَهِ فَهُوَ (۱) : أَنْ يُشَبَّهَ المُعَرَّفُ بِشَيْءٍ مَعْرُوفٍ (١) عِنْدَ السَّامِع ; لِقَصْدِ إِيْضَاحِ المُشَبَّهِ»; كَأَنْ يُشَبَّهَ العِلْمُ» بـ: «إِدْرَاكِ البَصَرِ»; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّ العِلْمَ هُوَ انْطِبَاعُ صُورَةِ المَعْلُومِ فِي البَصِيرَةِ، كَمَا أَنَّ إِدْرَاكَ البَصَرِ هُوَ انْطِبَاعُ صُورَةِ المُبْصَرِ فِي القُوَّةِ البَاصِرَةِ . وَمِنْهُ : ذِكْرُ جُزْئِيٌّ مِنْ جُزْئِيَّاتِ المُعَرِّفِ ; كَأَنْ يُقَالَ مَثَلاً : العِلْمُ كَإِدْرَاكِنَا أَنَّ الوَاحِدَ نِصْفُ الاِثْنَيْنِ.
As for definition by example (al-mithal), and it is definition by similitude, it is: that the defined is likened to something known to the listener, for the purpose of clarifying the resembled; such as likening 'knowledge' to 'visual perception'; meaning that knowledge is the imprinting of the image of the known in the inner sight, just as visual perception is the imprinting of the image of the visible in the power of sight. And from it (i.e., this type) is the mention of a particular from among the particulars of the definiendum; such as saying, for example: knowledge is like our perception that one is half of two.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage defines definition by example (al-ta'rif bi'l-mithal). The author explains that it involves comparing the defined to something familiar to the listener to clarify it. He gives an example: likening knowledge to visual perception; both involve the imprinting of an image (of the known or the visible) in the corresponding faculty (the inner sight or the power of sight). He then mentions a subtype: mentioning a particular instance of the definiendum, e.g., knowledge is like our knowledge that one is half of two. This is a form of definition by example that helps the listener grasp the concept through a concrete case.

Key Terms
تعريف بالمثال (ta'rif bi'l-mithal): Definition by comparing the defined to something known (similitude) or giving a particular instance. [Note: In later texts, this may be subsumed under ta'rif bi'l-tamthil.]
بصيرة (basira): Inner sight or intellect, the faculty that receives knowledge.

Definition by Division: Distinguishing the Defined Through Successive Divisions
Definition by division explained with a detailed example of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a definition by division for a given term from Islamic sciences and identify its genus and successive differentiae.

Core Idea
Definition by division eliminates alternatives to isolate what is uniquely true.

This lesson introduces a powerful method of definition used extensively in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh. Instead of listing properties, definition by division (al-ta'rif bi'l-qisma) works by starting with a broad genus and repeatedly dividing it based on essential differentiae until only the intended term remains. The author illustrates this with the definition of 'ilm (knowledge), showing how the process yields a precise result.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Starting Genus
The passage starts with 'belief' (al-i'tiqad). What is the broader category that the defined term belongs to? This genus must be a wider class that includes the definiendum and other things.
In the example, 'belief' is chosen as the genus for 'knowledge'.
2
Apply the First Division
The genus is divided into two mutually exclusive kinds: certain (jazim) vs. non-certain. The non-certain branch yields 'opinion' (zann), which is not knowledge. So we follow the certain branch.
Division must be exhaustive and exclusive.
3
Continue Subdividing
The certain branch is further divided: in accordance with reality (mutabiq) or not. The non-mutabiq is compound ignorance. Then the mutabiq branch is divided: firmly established (thabit) or not. The not firmly established is taqlid. The firmly established is knowledge.
Each division eliminates an alternative that shares characteristics with the definiendum.
4
Extract the Final Definition
After all divisions, the remaining branch yields the definition: 'certain belief, in accordance with reality, firmly established'. That is the complete definition of knowledge.
The definition is the conjunction of all the differentiae from the chosen branches.
5
Test for Completeness
Check if this definition includes all instances of knowledge and excludes everything else. Does it cover necessary knowledge, acquired knowledge, etc.? Does it exclude false beliefs, doubts, and unjustified confidence?
The author's definition passes this test in the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا التَّعْرِيفُ بِالقِسْمَةِ فَهُوَ : «تَمْيِيزُ المُعَرَّفِ عَمَّا يُشَارِكُهُ بِطَرِيقِ التَّقْسِيم»; كَأَنْ يُقَالَ مَثَلاً فِي تَعْرِيفِ العِلْمِ»: «الاعْتِقَادُ : إِمَّا جَازِمٌ، أَوْ غَيْرُ جَازِمِ. وَالثَّانِي : الظَّنُّ، وَالجَازِمُ : إِمَّا مُطَابِقٌ لِلوَاقِعِ أَو غَيْرُ مُطَابِقٍ، وَالثَّانِي : الجَهْلُ المُرَكَّبِ، وَالمُطَابِقُ : إِمَّا ثَابِتٌ لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقِيضَ، أَوْ غَيْرُ ثَابِتٍ ; الأَوَّلُ : العِلْمُ ، وَالثَّانِي : التَّقْلِيدُ، فَخَرَجَ مِنَ التَّقْسِيمِ : «أَنَّ العِلْمَ هُوَ : الاعْتِقَادُ الجَازِمُ المُطَابِقُ الثَّابِتُ)
As for definition by division, it is: 'distinguishing the defined from what shares with it by way of division'; such as saying, for example, in defining 'knowledge': 'Belief is either certain (jazim) or not certain. The second is opinion (zann). The certain is either in accordance with reality (mutabiq) or not in accordance. The second is compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). The one in accordance with reality is either firmly established (thabit) not admitting its opposite, or not firmly established. The first is knowledge ('ilm), and the second is blind following (taqlid). So from the division emerges: that knowledge is: the certain belief, in accordance with reality, firmly established.'
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage explains definition by division (al-ta'rif bi'l-qisma). The author describes it as a method of distinguishing the defined from things it shares attributes with, through a process of successive division (taqsim). He provides a detailed example for defining 'knowledge' ('ilm): Start with the genus 'belief' (i'tiqad). First division: belief is either certain (jazim) or not certain. The not certain is called 'opinion' (zann). The certain is then subdivided into that which is in accordance with reality (mutabiq) and that which is not (compound ignorance, jahl murakkab). Then the one in accordance with reality is further divided into that which is firmly established (thabit) and does not admit its opposite (this is 'knowledge') and that which is not firmly established (this is 'taqlid', blind following). Thus, by eliminating alternatives, we arrive at the definition: knowledge is certain belief, in accordance with reality, firmly established.

Key Terms
تعريف بالقسمة
اعتقاد جازم
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Definition by division isolates the unique attributes of a term by methodical elimination of alternatives, ensuring a precise and comprehensive definition.

Conditions for a Definition: Al-Iṭṭirād and Al-Inʿikās
Explains that every definition must be coextensive with the definiendum through two conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a definition in Islamic sciences satisfies the conditions of al-iṭṭirād (mutarid) and al-inʿikās (munʿakis).

Core Idea
A valid definition must exactly match the definiendum—no wider, no narrower.

In this passage, the commentator explains the two essential conditions that every definition must meet: mutarid (preventing non-instances) and munʿakis (comprehending all instances). Together they ensure the definition is coextensive with the definiendum, a cornerstone of precise conceptualization in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definition
Find the term being defined (al-muʿarraf) and the defining phrase (al-muʿarraf bi al-kasr) in the passage or in a given logical example.
In the text, the definiendum is the concept to be defined, and the definition is the phrase that attempts to capture it.
2
Check al-iṭṭirād – is the definition broader?
Ask: Are there individuals that satisfy the definition but do not satisfy the definiendum? If yes, the definition fails mutarid (it is aʿamm, more general).
Al-iṭṭirād is entailment from definition to definiendum: whenever the definition holds, the definiendum must hold.
3
Check al-inʿikās – is the definition narrower?
Ask: Are there individuals that satisfy the definiendum but do not satisfy the definition? If yes, the definition fails munʿakis (it is akhaṣṣ, more specific).
Al-inʿikās is the converse entailment: whenever the definiendum exists, the definition must exist.
4
Assess coextensiveness
If both conditions hold, the definition is coextensive (musāwin) with the definiendum. If either fails, the definition is invalid and must be revised.
The author emphasises that the definition must be neither more general, nor more specific, nor disjoint (mubāyin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرفات وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ : أَنْ يُرَى مُطَرِدَا مُنْعَكِسا، وَظَاهِراً لَا أَبْعَدَا وَلَا مُسَاوِياً، وَلَا تَجَوُّزَاء بِلَا قَرِينَةٍ بِهَا تُحُرِّزَا وَبَعْدَ الكَلَامِ عَلَى أَقْسَامِ المُعَرِّفِ، أَشَارَ إِلَى شُرُوطِهِ فَقَالَ: (وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ : أَنْ يُرَى مُطَرِدَا. مُنْعَكِساً) أَيْ : يُشْتَرَطُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُطَرِداً ; أَيْ: مَانِعاً ، وَأَنْ يَكُونَ مُنْعَكِساً; أَيْ: جَامِعاً ، وَذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ مُسَاوِياً لِلمُعَرَّفِ فِي الصِّدْقِ كَمَا مَرَّ ; أَيْ : أَنْ يَصْدُقَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - أَعَمُّ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ، وَلَا أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ، كَمَا لَا يَكُونُ مُبَايناً لَهُ بِالأَوْلَى . فَبِامْتِنَاعِ كَوْنِهِ أَعَمَّ يَكُونُ مُطَرِداً ، وَالاِطْرادُ هُوَ التَّلَازُمُ فِي النُّبُوتِ ; أَي : كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ الحَدُّ وُجِدَ المَحْدُودُ ; إذْ لَوْ كَانَ أَعَمَّ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودُ الأَخَص ، وَبِذَلِكَ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَدْخُلَ فِيهِ شَيْءٌ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ، فَيَكُونُ مَانِعاً . وَبِامْتِنَاعِ كَوْنِهِ أَخَصَّ يَكُونُ مُنْعَكِساً ، وَالمُرَادُ بِـ (الِانْعِكَاسِ : عَكْسُ المُرَادِ بِالِاطْرَادِ. وَفَسَّرَهُ العَضُدُ بِـ : «العَكْسِ المُسْتَوِي» ; أَي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المَحْدُودُ وُجِدَ الحَدُّ، فَيَلْزَمُ : أَنْ لَا يَخْرُجَ شَيْءٌ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ عَنِ الحَدِّ، فَيَكُونُ جَامِعاً .
Section on Definitions: Every definition is required to be seen as mutarid and mun'akis, manifest, not more remote, not equivalent, and without figurative language without a context that guards against it. After discussing the types of definition, he pointed to its conditions and said: 'Every condition is that it be seen as mutarid and mun'akis.' That is: it is required in every definition that it be mutarid, i.e., preventing, and that it be mun'akis, i.e., comprehensive. That is because it is necessary that the definition be equivalent to the definiendum in truth, as previously mentioned; i.e., each of them applies to every individual that the other applies to. So the definition is not more general than the definiendum, nor more specific, and a fortiori not disjoint from it. By preventing it from being more general, it becomes mutarid, and al-iṭṭirād is the entailment in predication; i.e., whenever the limit (definition) exists, the defined exists; because if it were more general, the existence of the more specific would not follow from its existence. And by that it necessitates that nothing enters into it from non-instances of the defined, so it is preventing. And by preventing it from being more specific, it becomes mun'akis, and by 'al-in'ikās' is meant the converse of what is meant by al-iṭṭirād. Al-'Aḍud interpreted it as 'the direct conversion'; i.e., whenever the defined exists, the definition exists; so it necessitates that nothing from the instances of the defined exits from the definition, so it is comprehensive.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage defines the two essential conditions for a valid definition in Islamic logic: al-iṭṭirād (اطّراد) and al-in'ikās (انعكاس). The author explains that a definition must be coextensive (مُساوٍ) with the definiendum; that is, it must apply to exactly the same individuals. The condition of being mutarid (مُطَّرِد) ensures that the definition is not wider than the definiendum: whenever the definition applies, the definiendum must apply. This prevents including any non-instances. The condition of being mun'akis (مُنْعَكِس) ensures that the definition is not narrower: whenever the definiendum applies, the definition must apply. This prevents excluding any instances. Together, they guarantee perfect mutual entailment. The author cites al-'Aḍud's interpretation of in'ikās as 'direct conversion' (العكس المستوي), meaning the logical converse of the entailment in iṭṭirād. For example, in Fiqh, defining 'wine' as 'intoxicating grape juice' is coextensive: every intoxicating grape juice is wine and every wine is intoxicating grape juice. A definition like 'intoxicating beverage' would be too wide (not mutarid) because it includes non-grape intoxicants. A definition like 'red grape juice' would be too narrow (not mun'akis) because it excludes white wine.

Key Terms
المعرف
المعرف بالكسر
مطرد
منعكس
اطراد
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A correct definition must be coextensive with the definiendum, satisfying both mutarid (no non-instances) and munʿakis (no missing instances).

Conditions of Muttarid and Mun'akis in Definition
Commentary explains the necessity of definiens being coextensive and defines muttarid and mun'akis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a definiens is coextensive with its definiendum by applying the conditions of muttarid (coextensiveness in affirmation) and mun'akis (reciprocity).

Core Idea
A valid definition must be both muttarid and mun'akis — the definiens must neither be more general nor more specific than the definiendum.

In this passage, the commentator explains that a proper definition requires the definiens to be equal in extension to the definiendum. The condition has two sides: muttarid ensures the definiens is not too broad, and mun'akis ensures it is not too narrow. Together, they guarantee that the definition exactly captures all and only the instances of the definiendum. This is a fundamental tool in Islamic logic for evaluating definitions used in fiqh, usul, and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate what is being defined (al-mu'arraf) and what is doing the defining (al-mu'arraf bi al-kasr). For example, if the definition is 'Human is a rational animal', the definiendum is 'human' and the definiens is 'rational animal'.
2
Check for muttarid (coextensiveness in affirmation)
Ask: Does every instance of the definiens also qualify as an instance of the definiendum? If the definiens is more general, then muttarid fails. For example, 'animal' is more general than 'human', so defining human as 'animal' violates muttarid.
Muttarid is defined as 'whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists'.
3
Check for mun'akis (reciprocity)
Ask: Does every instance of the definiendum also qualify as an instance of the definiens? If the definiens is more specific, then mun'akis fails. For example, 'rational' is more specific than 'human', so defining human as 'rational' violates mun'akis.
Mun'akis is defined as 'whenever the definiendum exists, the definiens exists' (al-'Adud's interpretation).
4
Verify the definition is coextensive
If the definiens passes both checks, it is coextensive (musawi) with the definiendum. If either fails, the definition is invalid. The passage emphasizes that the definiens must not be more general, more specific, or disparate.
The commentator corrects the wording to ensure the definitions apply to both hadd (real definition) and rasm (description).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُنْعَكِساً ؛ أَيْ: جَامِعاً ، وَذَلِكَ لأَنَّهُ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُعَرِّفُ مُسَاوِياً لِلمُعَرَّفِ فِي الصِّدْقِ كَمَا مَرَّ ؛ أَيْ : أَنْ يَصْدُقَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى كُلِّ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - أَعَمُّ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ، وَلَا أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ، كَمَا لَا يَكُونُ مُبَايناً لَهُ بِالأَوْلَى . فَبِامْتِنَاعِ كَوْنِهِ أَعَمَّ يَكُونُ مُطَرِداً ، وَالاِطْرادُ هُوَ التَّلَازُمُ فِي النُّبُوتِ ؛ أَي : كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ الحَدُّ وُجِدَ المَحْدُودُ ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ أَعَمُّ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودُ الأَخَص ، وَبِذَلِكَ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَدْخُلَ فِيهِ شَيْءٌ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ، فَيَكُونُ مَانِعاً . وَبِامْتِنَاعِ كَوْنِهِ أَخَصَّ يَكُونُ مُنْعَكِساً ، وَالمُرَادُ بِـ (الِانْعِكَاسِ : عَكْسُ المُرَادِ بِالِاطْرَادِ. وَفَسَّرَهُ العَضُدُ بِـ : «العَكْسِ المُسْتَوِي» ؛ أَي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المَحْدُودُ وُجِدَ الحَدُّ، فَيَلْزَمُ : أَنْ لَا يَخْرُجَ شَيْءٌ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ المَحْدُودِ عَنِ الحَدِّ، فَيَكُونُ جَامِعاً . قوله : (فَلَا يَكُونُ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - أَعَمُّ ... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ أنَّه إذا امتنع أن يكون أعم، وأن يكون أخصَّ أن لا يكون أعم من وجه أخص من وجه من باب أحرى؛ كتعريف «الإنسان» بـ «الأبيض» ؛ لفساد الطرد والعكس حينئذ. قوله : (أَيْ : كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ الحَدُّ وُجِدَ) صوابُهُ أن يقول : كلَّما وُجِدَ المعرِّفُ وُجِدَ المعرَّف؛ ليشمل ما إذا كان حدًّا ، وما إذا كان رسماً، وكذا يقال فيما بعده. قوله : (وَفَسَّرَهُ العَضُد هو عبد الرَّحمن بن أحمد بن عبد الغفار عضد الدين الشَّافعي، كان إماماً في المعقول مشاركاً في الفنون كثيرُ العِلم والمال والأنعام من أنجب تلامذته الكرماني والتفتازاني، له شرح على مختصر ابن الحاجب الأصلي»، و «المواقف»، و: «رسالة في المنطق، مات مسجوناً سنة ست وثمان مئة . قوله : (بِـ): «العَكْسِ المُسْتَوِي تبع العَضُد على هذا التفسير المولى سعد الدين، والجلال ا المحلي في شرح جمع الجوامع، غير أنَّ ما ذكره الشارح هنا يخالف ما سيأتي له من أنَّ العكس على هذا التفسير لم يوافق الاصطلاح ؛ اللَّهمَّ إلَّا أن يقال : ما هنا مبني على قول ابن أنَّ القضيَّة تنعكس كنفسها إذا كان المحمول مساوياً، وما يأتي على قول الجمهور هارون، من أنَّ الكلِّيَّة لا تنعكس إلَّا جزئيَّة؛ لأنَّ العكس هو ما كان وجه اللزوم. وَفَسَّرَهُ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ بِـ: «التَّلَازُمِ فِي الانْتِفَاءِ»؛ أَيْ : كُلَّمَا انْتَفَى الحَدُّ انْتَفَى المَحْدُودُ، وَذَلِكَ يَنْفِي كَوْنَ الحَدٌ أَخَصَّ؛ إذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ نَفْيِ الأَخَصُ نَفْيُّ الأَعَمِّ، فَمَالُهُمَا وَاحِدٌ . وَتَفْسِيرُ العَضُدِ مُوَافِقٌ فِي إِطْلَاقِ العَكْسِ لِلُّغَةِ وَلِلعُرْفِ أَيْضاً كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المَحَلِّيُّ؛ حَيْثُ قوله : (وَفَسَّرَهُ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ هو الفقيه المحصل أبو عمر عثمان بن الحاجب صاحب المختصرين العجيبين وغيرهما، توفي سنة وأربعين وست ،مئة وتبعه على هذا التفسير القطب والسَّنوسي، وما فسَّر به هو العكس لازمٌ لِمَا فسَّره به العضد ؛ لأنَّه عکس نقيضه الموافق، فالمخالفة بينهما إنَّما هي في العبارة فقط، وإلا فمالهما عند التأمل واحد كما ذكره الشارح؛ لأنَّ مرادهما أن لا يكون الحد أخصُّ، وكلا التفسيرين يقتضيه. واعترض العضد تفسير ابن الحاجب هذا بأنَّه تفسير للانعكاس بلازمه، لا ببيان مفهومه وحقيقته ؛ لأنه عكس نقيض المفهوم وعكس النقيض على ما سيأتي تبديل كل من طرفي القضية بنقيض الآخر مع بقاء الصدق والكيف. قوله : (لِلُّغَةِ) أي : لأنَّ العكس فيها مطلق التبديل والتحويل. قوله : (وَلِلعُرْفِ أَيْضاً أي: عرف التَّخاطب وحقيقته : تبديل طرفي القضيَّة مع بقاء الكيف والكم صدقاً وكذباً . قوله : (كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المَحَلَّيُّ) اسمُهُ : محمد بن أحمد، ولقبه : جلال الدين، كان قوَّالاً في الحقِّ لا تأخذه في الله لومة لائم، وكان يقول: «فهمي لا يقبل الخطأ، ولم يكن يقدر على الحفظ، وحفظ يوماً كرّاسة فامتلأ بدنه ،حرارة، كان يغلب عليه الملل وقلة الإقراء، وعُرض عليه القضاة فامتنع منه، توفي سنة أربع وستين وثمان مئة . وقد اعترض اللقاني قوله : «تفسير العضد موافق للعرف : بأنَّ هذا اشتباه؛ لأنَّ العرفيَّ إِنَّما يقع في جملة، والمدعى عكس الحد، ولا يتأتى فيه العرفي؛ لأنَّه لا يكون إِلَّا في قضيَّةٍ، وهذا مِنَ النَّاصر تحقيق دقيق، وإن أنكره تلميذه العبادي.
It is reciprocal, i.e., inclusive. This is because the definiens must be equal in truth to the definiendum, as passed; i.e., each must apply to every individual that the other applies to. So the definiens is neither more general than the definiendum nor more specific than it, just as it is not disparate from it a fortiori. By preventing it from being more general, it is coextensive (muttarid). Coextensiveness is mutual implication in affirmation, i.e., whenever the definition exists, the definiendum exists; because if it were more general, the existence of the more specific would not follow from its existence. And by that it follows that nothing from outside the individuals of the definiendum enters into it, so it is exclusive. By preventing it from being more specific, it is reciprocal (mun'akis). The intended meaning of reciprocity is the converse of the intended meaning of coextensiveness. Al-'Adud interpreted it as 'simple conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawi), i.e., whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists, so it follows that nothing of the individuals of the definiendum is excluded from the definition, so it is inclusive. His saying: (Then the definiens, with kasra, is not more general... etc.) Its apparent meaning is that if it is prevented from being more general and from being more specific, then a fortiori it is not more general in one respect and more specific in another; like defining 'human' as 'white', because then coextensiveness and reciprocity are invalid. His saying: (i.e., whenever the definition exists it exists) Its correct form is: whenever the definiens exists the definiendum exists, to include whether it is a definition (hadd) or a description (rasm), and similarly for what follows. His saying: (And al-'Adud interpreted it) He is 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn 'Abd al-Ghaffar 'Adud al-Din al-Shafi'i, he was an imam in rational sciences, participating in various arts, possessing much knowledge, wealth, and beneficence, among his prominent students were al-Kirmani and al-Taftazani. He has a commentary on Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib al-Asli, and al-Mawaqif, and a treatise on logic. He died imprisoned in the year 806. His saying: (with) 'simple conversion' following al-'Adud in this interpretation are al-Mulla Sa'd al-Din and al-Jalal al-Mahalli in his commentary on Jam' al-Jawami', except that what the commentator has mentioned here contradicts what will come from him that conversion according to this interpretation does not agree with the technical term, unless it is said that what is here is based on the statement of Ibn that the proposition converts as itself if the predicate is coextensive, and what comes is based on the statement of the majority (Jumhur) that a universal converts only as a particular, because conversion is what has the aspect of implication. And Ibn al-Hajib interpreted it as 'mutual implication in negation', i.e., whenever the definition is absent the definiendum is absent, and that negates the definition being more specific, because from the negation of the more specific the negation of the more general does not follow. So their intention is one. And al-'Adud's interpretation agrees with language and also with customary usage, as al-Mahalli mentioned. His saying: (And Ibn al-Hajib interpreted it) He is the accomplished jurist Abu 'Amr 'Uthman ibn al-Hajib, author of the two amazing compendia and others, died in the year 646. He was followed in this interpretation by al-Qutb and al-Sanusi. What he interpreted is the conversion necessary for what al-'Adud interpreted, because it is the conversion of its opposite that agrees. So the difference between them is only in expression; otherwise, their intention upon reflection is one, as the commentator mentioned, because their aim is that the definition not be more specific, and both interpretations require that. Al-'Adud objected to Ibn al-Hajib's interpretation by saying that it is an interpretation of reciprocity by its necessary consequence, not by explaining its concept and reality, because it is the conversion of the opposite of the concept, and conversion of the opposite will come later as replacing each of the two terms of the proposition with its opposite while preserving truth and quality. His saying: (for language) means because conversion in it is absolute substitution and transformation. His saying: (and also for customary usage) means the usage of discourse, and its reality is exchanging the two sides of the proposition while preserving quality and quantity, truth and falsehood. His saying: (as al-Mahalli mentioned) His name is Muhammad ibn Ahmad, his nickname Jalal al-Din. He was a speaker of truth, not fearing in God the blame of a blamer. He used to say: 'My understanding does not accept error, and he could not memorize. One day he memorized a notebook, and his body became full of heat. He was overcome by boredom and lack of teaching. He was offered judgeship but declined. He died in the year 864. Al-Laqani objected to his statement that 'al-'Adud's interpretation agrees with customary usage' by saying that this is confusion, because the customary usage [of conversion] only occurs in a proposition, and the claim is the conversion of the definition, and the customary [conversion] cannot be applied to it because it only occurs in a proposition. This is from al-Nasir a precise investigation, even if his student al-'Abbadi denied it.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the condition that a definition must be coextensive with the definiendum. The commentator is explaining the terms 'muttarid' (coextensive) and 'mun'akis' (reciprocal) which are two aspects of the requirement that the definiens and definiendum are equal in extension (musawi). The passage begins by stating that the definiens must be equal in truth to the definiendum, meaning they apply to exactly the same individuals. Therefore, the definiens cannot be more general (a'amm) because then it would include things that are not the definiendum; this would violate the condition of 'muttarid'. 'Muttarid' is defined as talazum fi al-nubut (mutual implication in affirmation): whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists. If the definiens were more general, the existence of the definiendum (the more specific) would not necessarily follow. This exclusivity is called 'mani'' (preventive). Conversely, if the definiens were more specific (akhass), it would not apply to all instances of the definiendum, violating 'mun'akis'. 'Mun'akis' is defined by al-'Adud as al-'aks al-mustawi (simple conversion): whenever the definiendum exists, the definiens exists. This ensures that the definiens is inclusive (jami'). The commentator then corrects a phrase: the original text said 'whenever the definition exists it exists' but it should say 'whenever the definiens exists the definiendum exists' to cover both hadd and rasm. The passage then includes a biographical note on al-'Adud: his full name, achievements, and that he died imprisoned in 806 AH. Next, the commentator discusses the interpretation of 'aks (conversion) by Ibn al-Hajib. Ibn al-Hajib defined mun'akis as talazum fi al-intifa' (mutual implication in negation): whenever the definiens is absent, the definiendum is absent. This also prevents the definiens from being more specific. The commentator notes that the two interpretations are equivalent in meaning, though expressed differently. Al-'Adud's interpretation uses the language of existence (wujud), while Ibn al-Hajib's uses negation (intifa'). The commentator then notes that al-Mahalli said al-'Adud's interpretation agrees with both linguistic and customary usage of 'aks. A biographical note on Ibn al-Hajib follows: he was Abu 'Amr 'Uthman ibn al-Hajib, died 646 AH. Al-'Adud objected to Ibn al-Hajib's interpretation, saying it explains reciprocity by its necessary consequence (lazim) rather than its true meaning, because it is essentially the conversion of the negative (i.e., contrapositive). The commentator then defines the customary 'aks (al-'aks al-'urfi) as swapping the two terms of a proposition while preserving quality, quantity, and truth value. Finally, a biographical note on al-Mahalli (Jalal al-Din Muhammad ibn Ahmad, died 864 AH) and an objection by al-Laqani that al-'Adud's interpretation does not match the customary conversion because the customary conversion applies only to propositions, not to definitions. Al-Laqani's point is subtle: the conversion of a definition (a term) is not the same as the conversion of a proposition. The commentator notes this is a precise investigation, though al-'Abbadi denied it.

Key Terms
mun'akis (منعكس)
muttarid (مطرد)
al-mu'arraf (المعرف)
al-mu'arraf bi al-kasr (المعرف بالكسر)
al-ittirād (الاطراد)
al-in'ikās (الانعكاس)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean for a definition to be 'muttarid'?
A) The definiens is more general than the definiendum.
B) Whenever the definiendum exists, the definiens exists.
C) Whenever the definiens exists, the definiendum exists.
D) The definiens and definiendum are completely unrelated.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition requires the definiens to be coextensive with the definiendum, which is secured by the two conditions of muttarid (no over-inclusion) and mun'akis (no under-inclusion).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage made from grapes' and test it for muttarid and mun'akis using the conditions from this lesson. Consider whether it covers all intoxicants (if the definiendum is 'intoxicant') or is too specific.
