Third Condition: Definiens Must Be Clearer Than Definiendum
Author explains the third condition for definitions with examples and resolves an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition violates the condition of clarity by comparing the definiens and definiendum, and resolve the objection of equal obscurity.

Core Idea
The definiens must be more apparent than the definiendum; obscurity or equality invalidates the definition.

In defining any term, the definiens must be clearer to the listener than the term being defined. This third condition prevents circular and obscure definitions. But what if both are equally unknown? The author addresses this puzzle with a distinction between current ignorance and ease of learning.

Guided Path
1
State the condition
The definiens must be more apparent (aẓhar) than the definiendum. This is the third condition of a valid definition.
2
Identify forbidden types
The definiens cannot be more remote (abʿad) i.e., more obscure, nor equal (musāwī) in obscurity. Examples: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' uses a more obscure term.
3
Analyze the objection
Objection: if both are unknown, how can one be more hidden? The author replies that equal ignorance does not imply equal difficulty in acquiring knowledge.
4
Apply the distinction
For someone who knows 'even', defining 'odd' as 'not even' is valid because 'even' is clearer for that person, even if both were originally unknown.
The condition is relative to the listener's knowledge.

وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَظَاهِراً لَا أَبْعَدَا ... وَلَا مُسَاوِياً») هَذَا هُوَ الشَّرْطُ الثَّالِثُ، أَيْ: يَجِبُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ ظَاهِراً ؛ أَي أَظْهَرَ مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ - بِالفَتْح ـ؛ أَيْ : أَجْلَى مِنْهُ، : وَأَوْضَحَ عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ ، فَلَا يَجُوزُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالأَبْعَدِ؛ أي: الأَخْفَى، وَلَا بِالمُسَاوِي لَهُ فِي الخَفَاءِ، فَالأَخْفَى كَتَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ» بـ: «أَنَّهَا جِسْمٌ كَالنَّفْسِ، وَالنَّفْسُ [٣٠/١] أَخْفَى مِنْهَا عِنْدَ العَقْلِ، وَتَعْرِيفُ الذَّهَبِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ النَّضَّارُه أَوِ) : «العَسْجَدُ»، وَالقَمَرِ بـ : «أَنَّهُ الزَّبْرَقَانِ»، وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِنَ الْأَلْفَاظِ الغَرِيبَةِ . فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : إِذَا كَانَ كُلٌّ مِنَ المُعَرِّفِ وَالمُعَرَّفِ مَجْهُولاً عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ، فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ عِنْدَهُ ب / ۲۸] ، فَكَيْفَ يَتَصَوَّرُ كَوْنَ أَحَدِهِمَا أَخْفَى عِنْدَهُ؟! قُلْتُ : هُمَا وَإِنِ اسْتَوَيَا فِي الجَهْلِ عِنْدَهُ ، قَدْ يَكُونُ الإِطْلَاعُ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا أَصْعَبُ وَالوُصُولُ إِلَى مَعْرِفَتِهِ أَشَقُّ، وَيَكُونُ الآخَرُ بِحَيْثُ يُتَوَصَّلُ إِلَى مَعْرِفَتِهِ بِأَقْرَبِ طَرِيقٍ . وَمِثَالُ المُسَاوِي فِي الخَفَاءِ : كَتَعْرِيفِ الزَّوْج» بـ : مَا لَيْسَ بِفَرْدٍ، وَعَكْسِهِ، وَهَذَا : بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِمَنِ اسْتَوَيَا عِنْدَهُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْ عَرَفَ أَنَّ الزَّوْجَ هُوَ : العَدَدُ المُنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ»، فَيَصِحُ أَنْ يُعَرَّفَ لَهُ الفَرْدُ بـ: مَا لَيْسَ بِزَوْجِ).
And his statement: (And apparent, not more remote... nor equal) this is the third condition, i.e.: it is required in every definiens that it be apparent; i.e. more apparent than the definiendum; i.e. clearer than it, and more evident to the listener. So definition by the more remote—i.e. the more hidden—is not permitted, nor by the equal to it in obscurity. An example of the more hidden is defining fire as 'a body like the soul', and the soul is more hidden than it in the intellect; and defining gold as 'al-nadhdhar' or 'al-ʿasjad', and the moon as 'al-zabraqān', and other such strange expressions. If you say: When both the definiens and definiendum are unknown to the listener, they are equal for him; so how can one of them be conceived as more hidden for him? I say: Even if they are equal in ignorance for him, it may be that gaining awareness of one is harder and reaching knowledge of it more difficult, while the other is such that knowledge of it is reached by the nearest path. An example of equals in obscurity is defining 'even number' as 'what is not odd', and its converse. This is relative to the one for whom they are equal. As for the one who knows that 'even' is the number divisible into two equal parts, then it is valid for him to define 'odd' as 'what is not even'.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition of a valid definition (muʿarraf): the definiens must be clearer than the definiendum. The author begins by stating that the definiens must be 'apparent' (zāhir), meaning more evident to the listener. It cannot be more obscure (abʿad) nor equal in obscurity. Examples of obscure definientia are given: defining fire as 'a body like the soul'—the soul is harder to grasp; defining gold as 'al-nadhdhar' (a rare word) or 'al-ʿasjad', and the moon as 'al-zabraqān' (foreign terms). Then an objection is raised: if both terms are unknown to the listener, they seem equal in obscurity, so how can one be more hidden? The answer is that equal ignorance does not mean equal difficulty in attaining knowledge. One term may be harder to learn or access, while the other is more easily approachable. For example, defining 'even' as 'not odd' and vice versa may be equal in obscurity for someone who understands neither. But if someone already knows that 'even' means divisible into two equal parts, then defining 'odd' as 'not even' becomes valid because 'even' is clearer for that person. Thus the condition is relative to the knowledge of the audience.

Key Terms
المُعَرِّف
المُعَرَّف
الأَظْهَر
الأَبْعَد
المُسَاوِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following definitions violates the condition that the definiens must be clearer than the definiendum?
A) Defining 'khamr' (wine) as 'muskir' (intoxicant) for a person who knows what intoxicates are.
B) Defining 'salat' (prayer) as 'rukuʿ wa sujud' (bowing and prostrating) for a layperson.
C) Defining 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) as 'sabr and taqsim' (exclusion and division) for someone unfamiliar with uṣūl al-fiqh.
D) Defining 'bayt' (house) as 'maskan' (dwelling) for an Arab child.

Key Takeaway
The clarity condition is audience-relative: a definiens must be more apparent than the definiendum for the specific listener, considering ease of learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a fiqh lesson, a student defines 'riba' (usury) as 'al-fadl al-mustanad ila al-illah' (excess based on the effective cause). Is this definition valid for a beginner? Identify which condition it violates and why.
?
Task 2: For a person who knows that 'wajib' means binding, would it be valid to define 'haram' as 'what is not wajib'? Use the author's reasoning about equal obscurity to answer.

The Fourth Condition: No Metaphor Without a Clear Clue
Author explains the fourth condition: avoid metaphor unless accompanied by a clear contextual clue (qarina).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a metaphorical definition meets the fourth condition of clarity by checking for a clear contextual clue.

Core Idea
Metaphor in definitions requires two clues: one for non-literality and one for intended meaning.

In this passage, the author lays down the fourth condition for a valid definition: the definiens must not employ metaphor unless a clear contextual clue (qarīna) is present that specifies the intended meaning. This rule prevents ambiguity in definitions. We will explore the condition, its examples, and the rationale behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify definiendum and definiens in each example
The passage gives two examples: tawaf defined as 'prayer without prostration, without ihram and salutation', and 'stupid person' defined as 'donkey'. Note the use of metaphor.
2
Locate the contextual clue (qarīna)
For tawaf, the clue is the phrase 'without prostration...' that specifies the meaning. For 'stupid person', the clue is the common understanding that 'donkey' is used metaphorically for dullness.
The clue must be 'wāḍiḥa' (clear).
3
Determine if the clue is clear
Both examples have clues explicit enough for the intended audience. The author emphasizes that the clue must specify the intended meaning (tuʿayyinu al-murād).
4
Apply the condition: is the metaphor acceptable?
Since clear clues are present, these definitions pass the fourth condition.
The author adds that the clue for non-literality is different from the clue for intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا تَجَوُّزَا... بِلَا قَرِينَةٍ بِهَا تُحُرِّزَا هَذَا هُوَ الشَّرْطُ الرَّابِعُ ؛ أَي : يَجِبُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ لَا يُرْتَكَبَ فِيهِ المَجَازُ بِلَا ظُهُورِ قَرِينَةٍ تُعَيِّنُ المُرَادَ مِنْهُ؛ كَتَعْرِيفِ «الطَّوَافِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ صَلَاةٌ دُونَ سُجُودٍ، وَلَا إِحْرَامٍ وَسَلَامٍ، وَكَتَعْرِيفِ البَلِيدِ) بـ: «الحِمَارِ»، فَإِنْ ظَهَرَتْ قَرِينَةٌ جَازَ. قَالَ السَّعْدُ : فَإِنْ قِيْلَ: المَجَازُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ ؛ لِكَوْنِهَا مَأْخُوذَةٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ؛ أَيْ : لِأَنَّهُمْ عَرَّفُوهُ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ اللَّفْظُ المُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي غَيْرِ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ تُصْرِفُهُ عَمَّا وُضِعَ لَهُ. قُلْنَا : هُوَ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ دَالَّةٍ عَلَى أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ لَمْ يُسْتَعْمَلْ فِيمَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، وَهِيَ غَيْرُ القَرِينَةِ الدَّالَّةِ عَلَى تَعِيينِ المُرَادِ . اهـ وَأَيْضاً : قَرِينَةُ المَجَازِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ قَدْ تَكُونُ خَفِيَّةٌ، وَقَرِينَنتُهُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ وَاضِحَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مَقْصُودَ التَّعْرِيفِ الإِيضاح . وَقَوْلُهُ : «تُحُرِّزا» مَبْنِيٌّ لِلمَفْعُولِ، وَالجُمْلَةُ فِي مَوْضِعِ جَرِّ صِفَةٍ لِقَرِينَةٍ .
And his statement: (And no metaphor... without a contextual clue by which one is protected) this is the fourth condition; i.e.: it is required in every definiens that figurative usage not be employed without a clear contextual clue that specifies the intended meaning from it; like defining 'tawaf' as 'a prayer without prostration, without ihram and salutation', and like defining 'stupid person' as 'donkey'. If a clue appears, it is permissible. Al-Saʿd said: If it is said: metaphor only occurs with a clue, because the clue is included in its definition; i.e., they defined it as 'a word used for other than what it was coined for, with a clue that turns it away from what it was coined for.' We reply: It only occurs with a clue that indicates that the word is not used for what it was coined for, but this is not the same as the clue that specifies the intended meaning. End quote. Also: The clue of metaphor as such may be hidden, but its clue in definitions must be clear, because the purpose of definition is clarification. And his word 'tuḥurrizā' is passive voice, and the clause is in the genitive position as a description of 'qarina'.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth condition for definitions: the definiens must not involve metaphor (majāz) unless there is a clear contextual clue (qarīna) that reveals the intended meaning. The author provides two examples: defining 'tawāf' (circumambulation) as 'prayer without prostration, without ihrām and salām'—here 'prayer' is figurative, but the qualification clarifies it. Another example: defining a dull person as 'donkey'—a metaphor. If the clue is clear, it's allowed. Then the author cites al-Saʿd al-Taftāzānī, who anticipates an objection: since the definition of metaphor itself includes a clue (it is a word used for other than its literal meaning with a clue), then any metaphor already has a clue; why require another? The answer: The clue that indicates the word is not used literally is different from the clue that specifies the intended figurative meaning. For metaphor to function, it only needs the first clue (non-literality), but for definition, we need the second clue (what the definiens actually means). Moreover, the clue of metaphor may be subtle or hidden, but the clue in a definition must be obvious because definition aims at clarity. Finally, the grammar of 'tuḥurrizā' is explained: it is passive, and the clause modifies 'qarīna'.

Key Terms
المَجَاز
القَرِينَة
الاسْتِعَارَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a second clue required in metaphorical definitions beyond the clue that indicates non-literal use?
A) Because metaphor always has two clues by definition.
B) Because the clue of non-literality does not specify the intended meaning, and definition requires unambiguous specification.
C) Because the author of the text requires two clues arbitrarily.
D) Because the clue of metaphor is always hidden and needs to be made clear.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical definitions are acceptable only if accompanied by a clear contextual clue that specifies exactly what the definiens means.

Two Types of Qarina: Non-Literality vs. Intended Meaning
Author clarifies the two types of qarina in metaphorical definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the qarina of metaphor (non-literality) and the qarina of intended meaning, and explain why the latter is necessary in definitions.

Core Idea
The clue that makes metaphor possible is not the same as the clue that makes it clear.

In the fourth condition discussion, al-Sa‘d al-Taftāzānī raises a subtle point: if metaphor by definition involves a clue, why require another? The reply reveals a crucial distinction: there are two types of qarina. One indicates that the word is not used literally; the other indicates the intended figurative meaning. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its significance for definitions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard definition of metaphor (majāz)
Metaphor includes a clue that turns away from literal meaning. This clue is part of the definition of majāz itself.
2
Identify the objection
Al-Sa‘d says: since every metaphor has a clue, why demand another clue for definitions?
3
Analyze the author's response
The author distinguishes: the clue that shows non-literal use is not the same as the clue that specifies the intended meaning. The latter is needed in definitions.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
For 'tawaf is prayer without prostration', the word 'prayer' is used non-literally (clue: we know prayer normally has prostration), but the additional phrase 'without prostration' specifies what is meant: a specific type of prayer (tawaf). Thus two clues.
This distinction prevents circularity in the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fifth Condition: No Circularity in Definition
Author states the fifth condition: definiens must not be known only through the definiendum, avoiding circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions and distinguish them from non-circular ones in Islamic legal and theological contexts.

Core Idea
A definition that depends on the thing it defines is like a compass that points to itself – it gives no direction.

The fifth condition for a valid definition is that the definiens must not be known only through the definiendum. This is the condition against circularity (dawr). In this lesson, we examine how the author explains this condition, including a subtle metaphorical use of the term 'maḥdūd' and the additional prohibition of homonyms without context clues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in the Arabic
Locate the phrase 'وَلاَ بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ' (and not by what is known only through the defined thing). This is the core condition against circularity. Underline it.
The phrase is part of a larger sentence; isolate it to see it clearly.
2
Understand the metaphorical use of 'maḥdūd'
Notice that the author uses 'maḥdūd' (literally 'the defined thing') to refer to the definiens. This is a metaphorical extension (majāz) where the more specific term stands for the more general. The definiens should be more known, but in circularity it is actually less known than the definiendum.
The phrase 'aṭlaqa al-muṣannif al-maḥdūd ʿalā muṭlaq al-muʿarrif' indicates this.
3
Connect to the prohibition of homonyms
The text also prohibits homonyms (mushtarak) without a clarifying clue. This relates to circularity: if a homonym is used and its intended meaning is only known through the definiendum, circularity arises. The clue (qarīna) must resolve the ambiguity independently.
This links the fifth condition to the earlier condition on clarity.
4
Test a real fiqhi example
Consider a definition of 'ḥarām' (forbidden) as 'what is not ḥalāl'. If the listener does not know 'ḥalāl' except through its opposite 'ḥarām', this is circular. Provide another example from fiqh, such as defining 'ṣalāh' as 'the action that invalidates without intention', which may be circular if 'invalidates' is understood only in terms of ṣalāh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : «وَلَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ»، وَلَا مُشْتَرَكِ مِنَ القَرِينَةِ خَلَا» وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ) هَذَا شَرْطُ خَامِسٌ ، وَأَطْلَقَ المُصَنِّفُ المَحْدُودَ عَلَى مُطْلَقِ المُعَرِّفِ مَجَازاً ؛ مِن اسْتِعْمَالِ الأَخَصُ فِي الأَعَمِّ بِقَرِينَةِ قَوْلِهِ : وَشَرْطُ كُلّ ... إلخ » .
And his statement: 'And not by what is known only through the defined thing, nor by a homonym devoid of contextual clue.' And his statement: 'And not by what is known only through the defined thing'—this is a fifth condition. The author used 'defined thing' (maḥdūd) metaphorically for absolute definiens, by using the more specific for the more general, with the clue of his statement: 'And the condition of every...' etc.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This brief passage introduces the fifth condition of definition: the definiens must not be something that can only be known through the definiendum itself. This is the condition against circularity (dawr). For example, if you define A by B, but B can only be understood through A, then the definition is useless. The author notes that the term 'maḥdūd' (the defined thing) is used here in a broader sense to refer to any definiens, not just the specific term being defined. This is a metaphorical extension (majāz) where the specific word is used for a general concept, as indicated by the phrase 'the condition of every...' which shows that the author is speaking generally of definientia. The condition also prohibits using homonyms (mushtarak) without a distinguishing clue, which connects back to the previous condition on clarity.

Key Terms
المَحْدُود
الدَّوْر
المُشْتَرَك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following definitions violates the fifth condition (no circularity)?
A) Defining 'wajib' (obligatory) as 'what one is rewarded for doing and punished for omitting'.
B) Defining 'haram' (forbidden) as 'what is not halal' for someone who does not know halal.
C) Defining 'salat' (prayer) as 'specific actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'.
D) Defining 'iman' (faith) as 'belief in the heart and profession on the tongue'.

Key Takeaway
The definiens must be more known than the definiendum; circular definitions defeat the purpose of definition.

?
Construct a non-circular definition for 'ribā' (usury) using genus and differentia, ensuring that neither the genus nor the differentia relies on the term 'ribā' or its synonyms.

Condition of Avoiding Metaphor Without Clear Qarina
Commentator explains the condition that definitions must avoid metaphor unless accompanied by a clear contextual indicator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a metaphorical definition is permissible by identifying the presence of a clear qarina.

Core Idea
Metaphor in definitions requires a clear qarina to clarify the intended meaning.

In this passage from al-Bajuri's commentary, the condition for using metaphor in definitions is examined. Through examples and a response to al-Taftazani's objection, the text clarifies that while metaphor inherently needs a qarina, the qarina in definitions must be particularly clear. This rule ensures that definitions achieve their goal of clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of metaphorical definitions
The passage gives two examples: defining tawaf as 'a prayer without prostration, ihram, and taslim', and defining al-balīd (dull-witted) as 'donkey'. Recognize that these use words in a non-literal sense.
2
Extract the condition stated
The condition is: if a contextual indicator (qarina) appears, such metaphorical definitions are permissible (فَإِنْ ظَهَرَتْ قَرِينَةٌ جَازَ). Note that without a clear qarina, they are not allowed.
3
Understand al-Sa'd's objection
Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) objects that metaphor always occurs with a qarina by definition, so the condition seems redundant. Locate this objection in the text: 'المَجَازُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ'.
4
Distinguish the two types of qarina
The commentator responds by distinguishing two qarinas: one that indicates the word is used metaphorically (which is inherent), and another that specifies the exact intended meaning. The inherent qarina may be hidden, but the clarifying qarina in definitions must be clear.
5
Explain why the qarina in definitions must be clear
The purpose of definition (ta'rīf) is clarification (īḍāḥ). Therefore, any qarina used must be clear, otherwise the definition would fail its purpose. This is why the condition is necessary despite the inherent qarina of metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطَّوَافِ بـ: «أَنَّهُ صَلَاةٌ دُونَ سُجُودٍ، وَلَا إِحْرَامٍ وَسَلَامٍ، وَكَتَعْرِيفِ البَلِيدِ) بـ: «الحِمَارِ»، فَإِنْ ظَهَرَتْ قَرِينَةٌ جَازَ.
قَالَ السَّعْدُ : فَإِنْ قِيْلَ: المَجَازُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ ; لِكَوْنِهَا مَأْخُوذَةٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ؛ أَيْ : لِأَنَّهُمْ عَرَّفُوهُ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ اللَّفْظُ المُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي غَيْرِ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ تُصْرِفُهُ عَمَّا وُضِعَ له.
قُلْنَا : هُوَ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ دَالَّةٍ عَلَى أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ لَمْ يُسْتَعْمَلْ فِيمَا وُضِعَ لَهُ، وَهِيَ غَيْرُ القَرِينَةِ الدَّالَّةِ عَلَى تَعِيينِ المُرَادِ . اهـ (۱) وَأَيْضاً : قَرِينَةُ المَجَازِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ قَدْ تَكُونُ خَفِيَّةٌ، وَقَرِينَنتُهُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ وَاضِحَةٌ ; لِأَنَّ مَقْصُودَ التَّعْرِيفِ الإِيضاح .
وَقَوْلُهُ : «تُحُرِّزا» مَبْنِيٌّ لِلمَفْعُولِ، وَالجُمْلَةُ فِي مَوْضِعِ جَرِّ صِفَةٍ لِقَرِينَةٍ .
[The definition] of tawaf by 'it is a prayer without prostration, without state of consecration (ihram), and without taslim', and [the definition] of al-balīd (dull-witted) by 'donkey'—if a contextual indicator (qarina) appears, it is permissible.
Al-Sa‘d said: For it is said: metaphor only occurs with a contextual indicator because it is included in its definition; i.e., because they defined it as 'a word used for something other than what it was originally coined for, with a contextual indicator that diverts it from what it was coined for.'
We say: It only occurs with a contextual indicator that indicates that the word is not used for what it was coined for, and that indicator is other than the indicator that specifies the intended meaning. End. And also: the contextual indicator of metaphor as such may be hidden, but its contextual indicator in the definition must be clear because the purpose of definition is clarification.
And his statement: 'tuḥarrizā' is passive voice, and the clause is in the genitive position as an adjective for 'qarina'.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage explains the condition that definitions must avoid metaphorical language unless accompanied by a clear contextual indicator (qarina). The commentator first gives examples: defining tawaf (circumambulation) as a prayer without prostration, ihram, and taslim is metaphorical; defining a dull-witted person as a donkey is also metaphorical. Such definitions are only permissible if a clear qarina is present. Al-Sa‘d (al-Taftazani) raises the objection that metaphor inherently requires a qarina, so the condition seems redundant. The commentator responds by distinguishing two types of qarina: one that indicates the word is not used in its literal sense (which is intrinsic to metaphor) and another that indicates the specific intended meaning (which may be hidden). In definitions, the latter qarina must be clear because the purpose of definition is clarification. Then the commentator notes the grammatical form of 'tuḥarrizā' (passive) indicating that the definition must be 'kept free' from metaphor without a clear qarina.

Key Terms
قَرِينَة
المَجَاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The metaphor must be well-known.
B) The definition must avoid any contextual indicator.
C) A clear contextual indicator (qarina) must be present.
D) The metaphor must be from the Qur'an or Hadith.

Key Takeaway
Metaphor is permissible in definitions only when accompanied by a clear contextual indicator, because the purpose of definition is clarification.

The Condition of Avoiding Circularity (Dawr) in Definitions
Commentator explains the condition of avoiding circularity with examples and al-Qarafi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions in Islamic texts, classify them as explicit or implicit, and evaluate whether they are permissible based on the audience and type of definition.

Core Idea
A definition must not rely on the term it defines, but al-Qarafi allows it in descriptive definitions if the audience knows one term.

In this lesson, we examine the key condition that a definition must avoid circularity (dawr). The commentator presents the rule, gives classic examples from Islamic sciences, then discusses al-Qarafi's nuanced view that audience knowledge can permit circularity in descriptive definitions. Finally, circularity is classified into explicit (muṣarraḥ) and implicit (muḍmar). Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing sound definitions in fiqh, kalām, and other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition statement
Find the phrase 'لا بما يدرى بمحدود' in the Arabic. This is the fifth condition: a definition must not be by what is known through the definiendum.
The commentator clarifies that 'definiendum' is used metaphorically for 'definer' in general.
2
Understand the logical impossibility
Circularity (dawr) is impossible because it requires each term to be both prior and posterior to the other, which is contradictory. Analyze why this is logically invalid.
The commentator calls it 'dawr muḥāl' (impossible circle).
3
Analyze the three examples
Trace the circular dependency in each example: (1) defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known' (the known depends on knowledge); (2) defining one correlative by the other (e.g., father by son); (3) defining 'sun' as 'daytime star' while daytime is defined by the sun's rising.
These are classic illustrations of explicit circularity.
4
Examine al-Qarafi's exception
Al-Qarafi argues that the prohibition is not absolute; it depends on the audience. If the listener knows one term (e.g., daytime) and not the other (sun), a circular definition can be instructive. The principle is to define the unknown by the known.
Verifiers restrict this to descriptive (rasm) and verbal definitions, not real definitions (ḥadd).
5
Distinguish explicit and implicit circularity
Circularity can be explicit (muṣarraḥ) without intermediaries, or implicit (muḍmar) through one or more steps. While implicit is harder to detect, explicit is more blameworthy due to its obviousness.
The commentator provides an example of implicit circularity involving the definition of 'two'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ) هَذَا شَرْطُ خَامِسٌ ، وَأَطْلَقَ المُصَنِّفُ المَحْدُودَ عَلَى مُطْلَقِ المُعَرِّفِ مَجَازاً ; مِن اسْتِعْمَالِ الأَخَصُ فِي الأَعَمِّ بِقَرِينَةِ قَوْلِهِ : وَشَرْطُ كُلّ ... إلخ ».
فصل في المعرفات
وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ فِيهِ لَفْظُ تَتَوَقَّفُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ - بِالفَتْحِ . ; لأَنَّهُ دَورٌ مُحَالٌ ; لِاقْتِضَائِهِ كَوْنَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مُتَقَدِّماً عَلَى الآخَرِ وَمُتَأَخِّراً عَنْهُ ; كَتَعْرِيفِ «العِلْمِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ مَعْرِفَةُ المَعْلُومِ، مَعَ تَوَقُفِ تَصَرُّرِ المَعْلُومِ عَلَى العِلْمِ، وَكَذِكْرِ أَحَدِ المُتَضَايِفَيْن كَالأُبُوَّةِ وَالبُنُوَّةِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الآخَرِ، وَكَتَعْرِيفِ الشَّمْسِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ كَوْكَبٌ نَهَارِيٌّ»، مَعَ كَوْنِ الشَّمْسِ مَأْخُوذَةٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّهَارِ ; لِأَنَّهُ : «الزَّمَنُ الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ» . وَذَكَرَ القَرَّافِيُّ أَنَّ المَنْعَ مِنْ هَذَا لَيْسَ عَلَى سَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ يَخْتَلِفُ بِحَسَبِ المُخَاطَبِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ المُخَاطَبُ يَعْرِفُ النَّهَارَ وَيَجْهَلُ الشَّمْسَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ لَهُ: «هِيَ : الكَوْكَبُ المُضِيءُ نَهَاراً ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَعْرِفُ الشَّمْسَ وَيَجْهَلُ النَّهَارَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هُوَ : الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ، قَالَ: وَالأَصْلُ فِي هَذَا البَابِ : أَنْ يُعْرِّفَ لِلسَّامِعِ مَا يَجْهَلُهُ بِمَا يَعْلَمُهُ . اهـ . وَذَلِكَ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ خَاصٌ بِالرُّسُومِ وَالتَّعْرِيفَاتِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ، كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ العَبَّادِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ. فَمَا أَوْرَدَهُ الشَّيْخُ يَحْيَى الشَّاوِيُّ عَلَى كَلَامِ القَرَّافِيِّ مِنْ: «أَنَّ الحَدَّ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ صَحِيحا فِي نَفْسِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ مُخَاطَبٍ دُونَ مُخَاطَبٍ ، وَإِلَّا كَانَتْ صِدَّتُهُ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ». اهـ (١) غَيْرُ وَارِدٍ ; لِأَنَّهُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيِّ (٢) ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ .
(۱) اهـ» زيادة من (أ) .
(۲) في (أ): زيادة «لَا الرُّسُومِ». 
.
ثمَّ الدَّور إما أن يكون مصرحاً ويسمى: «ظاهراً» أيضاً، وهو: «توقف الشَّيء على ما يتوقف عليه بلا واسطة كالمثل التي أتى بها أو مكنيا ويسمى أيضاً: «مضمراً» وهو: «توقف الشَّيء على ما يتوقف عليه بواسطة أو أكثر) كما يُقال : الاثنان أوّل عدد وينقسم بمتساويين، والمتساويان الشَّيئان الغير المتفاضلين، والشَّيئان الاثنان والمكنّي وإن كان في البادئ أخفى مِنَ المصرَّح، فالمصرح أشنع لظهوره.
And his statement: 'and not by what is known through the definiendum' — this is a fifth condition. The author used 'definiendum' metaphorically for the definer in general, using the specific for the general, with the indicator of his statement 'and the condition of every...' etc.
Section on Definitions
The meaning: In every definition it is necessary that there be no word whose knowledge depends on the definiendum (with fatḥ), because it is an impossible circle (dawr muḥāl), since it requires each to be prior and posterior to the other. Like defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known', while the conceptualization of the known depends on knowledge. And likewise mentioning one of the two correlatives, like fatherhood and sonship, in defining the other. And like defining 'sun' as 'a daytime star', while the sun is taken in the definition of 'daytime' because it is 'the time when the sun rises above the horizon'. Al-Qarafi mentioned that the prohibition of this is not by way of necessity, but rather it varies according to the audience. For if the audience knows the daytime and does not know the sun, it is correct to say to him: 'It is the bright star during the daytime.' And if he knows the sun and does not know the daytime, it is correct to say: 'It is that during which the sun rises above the horizon.' He said: The principle in this chapter is to define for the listener what he does not know by what he does know. End. And that, according to the verifiers, is specific to descriptions (rusūm) and verbal definitions (ta‘rīfāt lafẓiyya), as verified by al-‘Abbādī and others. So what Shaykh Yaḥyā al-Shāwī objected to al-Qarāfī’s statement — that 'the definition (ḥadd) must be sound in itself, regardless of the audience, otherwise its validity would depend on something else' — is unfounded, because it is based on that being about real definition (ta‘rīf ḥaqīqī), which is not the case.
Then circularity is either explicit (muṣarraḥ), also called 'apparent' (ẓāhir), which is 'the dependence of a thing on what it depends on without intermediation', like the examples given; or implicit (muknī), also called 'hidden' (muḍmar), which is 'the dependence of a thing on what it depends on through one or more intermediaries', as is said: 'Two is the first number that can be divided into two equal parts', and 'the two equal parts are two things that are not disparate', and 'the two things are two'. The implicit, even if at first glance it is more hidden than the explicit, the explicit is uglier due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that a definition must not involve circularity (dawr). The commentator first identifies the poet's phrase 'لا بما يدرى بمحدود' as the fifth condition. He then explains that the poet used 'definiendum' (al-maḥdūd) metaphorically for the definer in general. The core meaning is that in every definition, there must be no word whose understanding depends on the definiendum itself, because that would create an impossible circularity where each term is both prior and posterior to the other. Three examples illustrate this: defining knowledge as 'cognition of the known' while the known depends on knowledge; defining one correlative (e.g., father) by the other (son); and defining the sun as 'a daytime star' while daytime is defined by the sun's rising. The commentator then presents al-Qarafi's nuanced view: the prohibition is not absolute but relative to the audience. If the audience knows one term but not the other, circularity in description may be permissible. However, the verifiers restrict this to descriptive definitions (rusūm) and verbal definitions (ta‘rīfāt lafẓiyya), not to real definitions (ḥadd). The objection of Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi is dismissed as it mistakenly assumes the definitions in question are real. Finally, circularity is classified into explicit (direct) and implicit (through intermediaries). Explicit circularity is more obvious and therefore more blameworthy, while implicit is harder to detect but still problematic.

Key Terms
الدَّور
مُصَرَّح
مُضْمَر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of explicit circularity given in the passage?
A) Defining 'two' as 'the first number that can be divided into two equal parts' with subsequent dependencies.
B) Defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known'.
C) Defining 'father' using 'son' when the listener knows neither.
D) Defining 'sun' as 'the bright star during the daytime' for someone who knows daytime.

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definitions is logically invalid when independence is required, but in descriptive and verbal definitions, it may be permissible if it helps the audience learn a new concept from a known one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition 'Wine (khamr) is an intoxicating beverage that intoxicates' is circular. Identify which term is the definiendum and which terms depend on it. Then rewrite the definition to avoid circularity using a different differentia.
?
Al-Qarafi allows circular definition in descriptive definitions if the audience knows one term. Give an example from Islamic sciences where a descriptive definition might appropriately use a circular reference to teach a new concept.

Avoiding Circularity in Definitions: The Core Principle
Explains the condition of avoiding circularity in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions in Islamic texts and explain why they are logically impossible.

Core Idea
A circular definition traps you in an impossible loop: each term depends on the other.

This lesson introduces the fundamental condition that a valid definition must avoid circularity (dawr). The passage explains that a definer cannot contain a term whose understanding depends on the defined term, as this creates an impossible logical circle where each term must precede and succeed the other simultaneously. Through concrete examples from the Islamic sciences, you will learn to spot this fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definer
Locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the expression used to define it (definer). In the examples, 'knowledge', 'sun', and 'father' are definienda.
2
Check for dependency
Examine whether any term in the definer requires prior knowledge of the definiendum. For example, 'known' depends on 'knowledge'.
3
Determine the type of circularity
If the dependency is direct (e.g., A depends on B, and B depends on A), it is explicit circularity. If mediated, it is implicit.
This step anticipates the classification in later lessons.
4
Conclude validity
If circularity is present, the definition is logically flawed and must be rejected in proper definitions.

Source Text

والمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ فِيهِ لَفْظُ تَتَوَقَّفُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ - بِالفَتْحِ . ؛ لأَنَّهُ دَورٌ مُحَالٌ ؛ لِاقْتِضَائِهِ كَوْنَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مُتَقَدِّماً عَلَى الآخَرِ وَمُتَأَخِّراً عَنْهُ ؛ كَتَعْرِيفِ «العِلْمِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ مَعْرِفَةُ المَعْلُومِ، مَعَ تَوَقُفِ تَصَوُّرِ المَعْلُومِ عَلَى العِلْمِ، وَكَذِكْرِ أَحَدِ المُتَضَايِفَيْن كَالأُبُوَّةِ وَالبُنُوَّةِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الآخَرِ، وَكَتَعْرِيفِ الشَّمْسِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ كَوْكَبٌ نَهَارِيٌّ»، مَعَ كَوْنِ الشَّمْسِ مَأْخُوذَةٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّهَارِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ : «الزَّمَنُ الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ» . وَذَكَرَ القَرَّافِيُّ أَنَّ المَنْعَ مِنْ هَذَا لَيْسَ عَلَى سَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ يَخْتَلِفُ بِحَسَبِ المُخَاطَبِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ المُخَاطَبُ يَعْرِفُ النَّهَارَ وَيَجْهَلُ الشَّمْسَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ لَهُ: «هِيَ : الكَوْكَبُ المُضِيءُ نَهَاراً ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَعْرِفُ الشَّمْسَ وَيَجْهَلُ النَّهَارَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هُوَ : الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ، قَالَ: وَالأَصْلُ فِي هَذَا البَابِ : أَنْ يُعَرِّفَ لِلسَّامِعِ مَا يَجْهَلُهُ بِمَا يَعْلَمُهُ . اهـ . وَذَلِكَ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ خَاصٌ بِالرُّسُومِ وَالتَّعْرِيفَاتِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ، كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ العَبَّادِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ. فَمَا أَوْرَدَهُ الشَّيْخُ يَحْيَى الشَّاوِيُّ عَلَى كَلَامِ القَرَّافِيِّ مِنْ: «أَنَّ الحَدَّ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ صَحِيحا فِي نَفْسِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ مُخَاطَبٍ دُونَ مُخَاطَبٍ ، وَإِلَّا كَانَتْ صِدَّتُهُ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ». غَيْرُ وَارِدٍ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيِّ ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ . ثُمَّ الدَّور إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُصَرَّحاً وَيُسَمَّى: «ظَاهِراً» أَيْضاً، وَهُوَ: «تَوَقُفُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ كَالْمِثْلِ الَّتِي أَتَى بِهَا أَوْ مَكْنِيّاً وَيُسَمَّى أَيْضاً: «مُضْمَراً» وَهُوَ: «تَوَقُفُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ بِوَاسِطَةٍ أَوْ أَكْثَرَ) كَمَا يُقَالُ : الاثْنَانِ أَوَّلُ عَدَدٍ وَيَنْقَسِمُ بِمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَالمُتَسَاوِيَانِ الشَّيْئَانِ الغَيْرُ المُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ، وَالشَّيْئَانِ الاثْنَانِ وَالمَكْنِيُّ وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي البَادِئِ أَخْفَى مِنَ المُصَرَّحِ، فَالُمصَرَّحُ أَشْنَعُ لِظُهُورِهِ.
Chapter on Definitions. The meaning: It is necessary in every definer that there is no word whose knowledge depends on the defined (object) — with fatha; because it is an impossible circularity; because it requires each of them to be prior to the other and posterior to it; like defining 'knowledge' as 'it is the cognition of the known, with the conception of the known depending on knowledge', and like mentioning one of the two correlatives such as fatherhood and sonship in the definition of the other, and like defining 'sun' as 'it is a daytime planet', while the sun is taken in the definition of daytime because it is 'the time in which the sun rises above the horizon'. And al-Qarafi mentioned that the prohibition of this is not necessarily absolute, but differs according to the addressee. If the addressee knows daytime and is ignorant of the sun, it is correct to say to him: 'It is the shining planet during daytime', and if he knows the sun and is ignorant of daytime, it is correct to say: 'It is that in which the sun rises above the horizon'. He said: 'The principle in this chapter is to define for the listener what he is ignorant of by what he knows.' End. And that, according to the verifiers, is specific to descriptions (rusum) and verbal definitions (ta'rifat lafziyya), as al-'Abbadi and others verified. Thus what Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi objected to al-Qarafi's statement: 'The definition (hadd) must be correct in itself, disregarding one addressee or another, otherwise its validity would depend on something else' — is not valid because it is based on that being in real definitions (ta'rif haqiqi), and it is not so. Then circularity is either explicit (musarrah) and also called 'apparent' (zahir), which is: 'a thing's dependence on that upon which it depends without intermediary, like the examples he gave'; or implicit (makni) also called 'concealed' (mudmar), which is: 'a thing's dependence on that upon which it depends with one or more intermediaries', as it is said: 'Two is the first number, and it is divisible into equals; equals are two things that are not superior to each other; two things are two.' The implicit, though more hidden initially than the explicit, the explicit is uglier due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage defines the condition that a definition must not contain circularity. It begins by stating the principle: every definer (mu'arrij) must avoid any word whose understanding depends on the defined term (mu'arraf). This leads to impossible circularity (dawr muhal), where each term would have to precede and succeed the other simultaneously. Three examples illustrate: (1) defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known' while the conception of the known depends on knowledge; (2) defining correlatives like father and son in terms of each other; (3) defining 'sun' as 'daytime planet' while daytime is defined as 'the time when the sun rises above the horizon'. Al-Qarafi then provides a qualification: this prohibition is not absolute but depends on the audience. If the audience knows daytime but not the sun, defining the sun as 'the shining planet during daytime' is valid; conversely, if they know the sun but not daytime, defining daytime as 'that in which the sun rises' is valid. The principle is to teach the unknown via the known. However, the verification scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) restrict this to descriptive definitions (rusum) and verbal definitions (al-ta'rifat al-lafziyya), not real definitions (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi). This responds to a criticism by Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi, who argued that a definition must be valid in itself regardless of the audience. The response is that al-Shawi's objection only applies to real definitions, not the type al-Qarafi is discussing. Finally, the passage classifies circularity into two types: explicit (musarrah) which is directly circular, and implicit (makni or mudmar) which involves a chain of dependence. An example of implicit circularity: 'Two is the first number divisible into equals; equals are two things not exceeding each other; two things are two.' Though implicit is initially more hidden, explicit circularity is worse because it is obvious.

معرف

According to the passage, which of the following is an example of circularity (دور) in definition?
A) Defining 'water' as 'H2O'
B) Defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known'
C) Defining 'triangle' as 'three-sided polygon'
D) Defining 'man' as 'rational animal'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must not contain any term whose understanding presupposes knowledge of the defined term itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the circularity in the definition: 'العلم هو معرفة المعلوم' where 'المعلوم' depends on 'العلم'. Explain why it is circular.
?
Construct a non-circular definition for 'sunnah' in hadith terminology that does not involve 'prophet' defined in terms of his sunnah.

Module 3 · Lesson 298

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Al-Qarafi's Audience-Relative Approach to Circularity
Explains al-Qarafi's qualification on circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Qarafi's principle that circularity is permissible when the audience knows the unknown via the known, and distinguish this from real definitions.

Core Idea
Al-Qarafi allows circular definitions for teaching, but only in descriptive and verbal definitions.

This lesson examines al-Qarafi's nuanced position: the prohibition of circularity is not absolute but depends on the audience. If the audience knows the term used in the definer, even if that term depends on the definiendum, the definition can be pedagogically valid. However, verifiers restrict this to descriptive and verbal definitions, not real definitions that capture essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Qarafi's claim
He states that the prohibition of circularity is not necessary (لزومي) but differs by the addressee. If the addressee knows 'daytime', defining 'sun' as 'the shining planet during daytime' is valid.
This is based on the principle: define the unknown by the known.
2
Recognize the restriction by verifiers
Al-'Abbadi and other muhaqqiqun specify that this only applies to rusum (descriptions) and ta'rifat lafziyya (verbal definitions), not al-ta'rif al-haqiqi (real definitions).
Real definitions must be absolutely non-circular.
3
Respond to al-Shawi's objection
Al-Shawi argued that a definition must be correct in itself regardless of audience. The passage responds that this applies to real definitions, not the type al-Qarafi discusses.
This shows the importance of classifying definitions.

Source Text

أَشْنَعُ لِظُهُورِهِ.
Chapter on Definitions. The meaning: It is necessary in every definer that there is no word whose knowledge depends on the defined (object) — with fatha; because it is an impossible circularity; because it requires each of them to be prior to the other and posterior to it; like defining 'knowledge' as 'it is the cognition of the known, with the conception of the known depending on knowledge', and like mentioning one of the two correlatives such as fatherhood and sonship in the definition of the other, and like defining 'sun' as 'it is a daytime planet', while the sun is taken in the definition of daytime because it is 'the time in which the sun rises above the horizon'. And al-Qarafi mentioned that the prohibition of this is not necessarily absolute, but differs according to the addressee. If the addressee knows daytime and is ignorant of the sun, it is correct to say to him: 'It is the shining planet during daytime', and if he knows the sun and is ignorant of daytime, it is correct to say: 'It is that in which the sun rises above the horizon'. He said: 'The principle in this chapter is to define for the listener what he is ignorant of by what he knows.' End. Commentary
This passage defines the condition that a definition must not contain circularity. It begins by stating the principle: every definer (mu'arrij) must avoid any word whose understanding depends on the defined term (mu'arraf). This leads to impossible circularity (dawr muhal), where each term would have to precede and succeed the other simultaneously. Three examples illustrate: (1) defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known' while the conception of the known depends on knowledge; (2) defining correlatives like father and son in terms of each other; (3) defining 'sun' as 'daytime planet' while daytime is defined as 'the time when the sun rises above the horizon'. Al-Qarafi then provides a qualification: this prohibition is not absolute but depends on the audience. If the audience knows daytime but not the sun, defining the sun as 'the shining planet during daytime' is valid; conversely, if they know the sun but not daytime, defining daytime as 'that in which the sun rises' is valid. The principle is to teach the unknown via the known. However, the verification scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) restrict this to descriptive definitions (rusum) and verbal definitions (al-ta'rifat al-lafziyya), not real definitions (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi). This responds to a criticism by Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi, who argued that a definition must be valid in itself regardless of the audience. The response is that al-Shawi's objection only applies to real definitions, not the type al-Qarafi is discussing. Finally, the passage classifies circularity into two types: explicit (musarrah) which is directly circular, and implicit (makni or mudmar) which involves a chain of dependence. An example of implicit circularity: 'Two is the first number divisible into equals; equals are two things not exceeding each other; two things are two.' Though implicit is initially more hidden, explicit circularity is worse because it is obvious.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers (المحققين), al-Qarafi's audience-relative approach is restricted to which type of definitions?
A) Real definitions (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi)
B) Descriptive and verbal definitions (al-rusum wa al-ta'rifat al-lafziyya)
C) All definitions without exception
D) Only definitions in Kalam

Key Takeaway
Al-Qarafi's principle that circularity is acceptable when defining by the known applies only to descriptive and verbal definitions, not real definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate: A teacher defines 'ghusl' as 'washing the body with water', but the student does not know 'washing' in the ritual sense. Is this definition valid according to al-Qarafi? Why or why not?
?

Explicit vs. Implicit Circularity
Classifies circularity into explicit and implicit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and implicit circularity and evaluate which is more problematic.

Core Idea
Explicit circularity is direct and obvious; implicit circularity hides in a chain of dependencies.

This lesson classifies circularity into two types: explicit (musarrah), where a term depends directly on another that depends on it without intermediary, and implicit (makni/mudmar), where the dependence is mediated by one or more terms. The passage provides an example of implicit circularity using the definitions of 'two' and 'equals'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pattern of dependence
In explicit circularity, A depends on B, and B depends on A directly. In implicit circularity, A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A (or longer chain).
The passage gives the example: Two is defined by equals, equals by two things, two things by two.
2
Classify the example from the text
The definition of 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known' is explicit because the dependency is direct. The definition of 'two' is implicit because it involves three terms.
3
Compare severity
The passage states that explicit circularity is uglier (أشنع) because it is obvious, while implicit is more hidden but less severe in terms of logical error.
Both are invalid in real definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرفات
والمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ يَجِبُ فِي كُلِّ مُعَرِّفٍ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ فِيهِ لَفْظُ تَتَوَقَّفُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ - بِالفَتْحِ . ؛ لأَنَّهُ دَورٌ مُحَالٌ ؛ لِاقْتِضَائِهِ كَوْنَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مُتَقَدِّماً عَلَى الآخَرِ وَمُتَأَخِّراً عَنْهُ ؛ كَتَعْرِيفِ «العِلْمِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ مَعْرِفَةُ المَعْلُومِ، مَعَ تَوَقُفِ تَصَوُّرِ المَعْلُومِ عَلَى العِلْمِ، وَكَذِكْرِ أَحَدِ المُتَضَايِفَيْن كَالأُبُوَّةِ وَالبُنُوَّةِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الآخَرِ، وَكَتَعْرِيفِ الشَّمْسِ» بـ: «أَنَّهُ كَوْكَبٌ نَهَارِيٌّ»، مَعَ كَوْنِ الشَّمْسِ مَأْخُوذَةٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّهَارِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ : «الزَّمَنُ الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ» . وَذَكَرَ القَرَّافِيُّ أَنَّ المَنْعَ مِنْ هَذَا لَيْسَ عَلَى سَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ يَخْتَلِفُ بِحَسَبِ المُخَاطَبِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ المُخَاطَبُ يَعْرِفُ النَّهَارَ وَيَجْهَلُ الشَّمْسَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ لَهُ: «هِيَ : الكَوْكَبُ المُضِيءُ نَهَاراً ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَعْرِفُ الشَّمْسَ وَيَجْهَلُ النَّهَارَ صَحَ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هُوَ : الَّذِي تَطْلُعُ فِيهِ الشَّمْسُ فَوْقَ الأُفُقِ، قَالَ: وَالأَصْلُ فِي هَذَا البَابِ : أَنْ يُعَرِّفَ لِلسَّامِعِ مَا يَجْهَلُهُ بِمَا يَعْلَمُهُ . اهـ . وَذَلِكَ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ خَاصٌ بِالرُّسُومِ وَالتَّعْرِيفَاتِ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ، كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ العَبَّادِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ. فَمَا أَوْرَدَهُ الشَّيْخُ يَحْيَى الشَّاوِيُّ عَلَى كَلَامِ القَرَّافِيِّ مِنْ: «أَنَّ الحَدَّ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ صَحِيحا فِي نَفْسِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ مُخَاطَبٍ دُونَ مُخَاطَبٍ ، وَإِلَّا كَانَتْ صِدَّتُهُ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ». غَيْرُ وَارِدٍ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيِّ ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ . ثُمَّ الدَّور إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُصَرَّحاً وَيُسَمَّى: «ظَاهِراً» أَيْضاً، وَهُوَ: «تَوَقُفُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ كَالْمِثْلِ الَّتِي أَتَى بِهَا أَوْ مَكْنِيّاً وَيُسَمَّى أَيْضاً: «مُضْمَراً» وَهُوَ: «تَوَقُفُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ بِوَاسِطَةٍ أَوْ أَكْثَرَ) كَمَا يُقَالُ : الاثْنَانِ أَوَّلُ عَدَدٍ وَيَنْقَسِمُ بِمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَالمُتَسَاوِيَانِ الشَّيْئَانِ الغَيْرُ المُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ، وَالشَّيْئَانِ الاثْنَانِ وَالمَكْنِيُّ وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي البَادِئِ أَخْفَى مِنَ المُصَرَّحِ، فَالُمصَرَّحُ أَشْنَعُ لِظُهُورِهِ.
Chapter on Definitions. The meaning: It is necessary in every definer that there is no word whose knowledge depends on the defined (object) — with fatha; because it is an impossible circularity; because it requires each of them to be prior to the other and posterior to it; like defining 'knowledge' as 'it is the cognition of the known, with the conception of the known depending on knowledge', and like mentioning one of the two correlatives such as fatherhood and sonship in the definition of the other, and like defining 'sun' as 'it is a daytime planet', while the sun is taken in the definition of daytime because it is 'the time in which the sun rises above the horizon'. And al-Qarafi mentioned that the prohibition of this is not necessarily absolute, but differs according to the addressee. If the addressee knows daytime and is ignorant of the sun, it is correct to say to him: 'It is the shining planet during daytime', and if he knows the sun and is ignorant of daytime, it is correct to say: 'It is that in which the sun rises above the horizon'. He said: 'The principle in this chapter is to define for the listener what he is ignorant of by what he knows.' End. And that, according to the verifiers, is specific to descriptions (rusum) and verbal definitions (ta'rifat lafziyya), as al-'Abbadi and others verified. Thus what Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi objected to al-Qarafi's statement: 'The definition (hadd) must be correct in itself, disregarding one addressee or another, otherwise its validity would depend on something else' — is not valid because it is based on that being in real definitions (ta'rif haqiqi), and it is not so. Then circularity is either explicit (musarrah) and also called 'apparent' (zahir), which is: 'a thing's dependence on that upon which it depends without intermediary, like the examples he gave'; or implicit (makni) also called 'concealed' (mudmar), which is: 'a thing's dependence on that upon which it depends with one or more intermediaries', as it is said: 'Two is the first number, and it is divisible into equals; equals are two things that are not superior to each other; two things are two.' The implicit, though more hidden initially than the explicit, the explicit is uglier due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage defines the condition that a definition must not contain circularity. It begins by stating the principle: every definer (mu'arrij) must avoid any word whose understanding depends on the defined term (mu'arraf). This leads to impossible circularity (dawr muhal), where each term would have to precede and succeed the other simultaneously. Three examples illustrate: (1) defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition of the known' while the conception of the known depends on knowledge; (2) defining correlatives like father and son in terms of each other; (3) defining 'sun' as 'daytime planet' while daytime is defined as 'the time when the sun rises above the horizon'. Al-Qarafi then provides a qualification: this prohibition is not absolute but depends on the audience. If the audience knows daytime but not the sun, defining the sun as 'the shining planet during daytime' is valid; conversely, if they know the sun but not daytime, defining daytime as 'that in which the sun rises' is valid. The principle is to teach the unknown via the known. However, the verification scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) restrict this to descriptive definitions (rusum) and verbal definitions (al-ta'rifat al-lafziyya), not real definitions (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi). This responds to a criticism by Shaykh Yahya al-Shawi, who argued that a definition must be valid in itself regardless of the audience. The response is that al-Shawi's objection only applies to real definitions, not the type al-Qarafi is discussing. Finally, the passage classifies circularity into two types: explicit (musarrah) which is directly circular, and implicit (makni or mudmar) which involves a chain of dependence. An example of implicit circularity: 'Two is the first number divisible into equals; equals are two things not exceeding each other; two things are two.' Though implicit is initially more hidden, explicit circularity is worse because it is obvious.

Key Terms
دور
مصرح
مكني / مضمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage consider explicit circularity (المصرح) more problematic than implicit (المكني)?
A) Because it is more common in the tradition
B) Because it is obvious and thus more disgraceful in argumentation
C) Because it involves more terms
D) Because it can never be resolved

Key Takeaway
Explicit circularity is directly visible and therefore more disgraceful, while implicit circularity is hidden but also invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the circularity in the following chain: 'Ijtihad is defined as the maximal effort of the faqih in seeking a ruling; faqih is one who performs ijtihad.' Is this explicit or implicit? Why?
?
Construct a chain of three terms that leads to implicit circularity using Islamic legal terms (e.g., 'fiqh', 'hukm', 'mujtahid').

Condition Six: Avoiding Ambiguous Words Without Context
Explains the condition that ambiguous words must not be used in definitions unless accompanied by a clarifying context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an ambiguous word (mushtarak) may be used in a definition and specify the required clarifying context (qarina).

Core Idea
Ambiguity in definitions is forbidden unless context clarifies the intended meaning.

In defining terms, clarity is paramount. The logicians therefore set conditions to prevent ambiguity. One such condition is that the definer must avoid ambiguous words (mushtarak) unless accompanied by a context that specifies the meaning. This lesson unpacks that condition and its exceptions, showing how even a word like 'العين' can be used legitimately when context is provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous word (mushtarak) in a definition
Recognize that a word has multiple distinct meanings. For example, 'العين' can mean eye, spring, or gold coin. Without context, the intended meaning is unclear.
2
Determine if a clarifying context (qarina) is present
Check if additional wording specifies the intended sense. For instance, saying 'العين أي النضار' clarifies that 'العين' means 'the gold coin'.
3
Apply the analogy to metaphor (majaz) as cited from al-Ghazali and al-Qarafi
Note that the same rule applies to figurative language: without context, it is forbidden. The reasoning is that definition aims at clarity.
Al-Qarafi extended al-Ghazali's ruling on metaphor to ambiguous words.
4
Test the condition on an example like defining 'الأبيض' using 'الحيوان'
Recognize that 'الحيوان' is not ambiguous per se, but when used to define 'الأبيض', it is too vague because 'الأبيض' could be anything white. Adding 'المفرق للبصر' (distinguishing sight) clarifies it is the eye.
This example is included via the word 'مثلاً' in the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا مُشْتَرَكِ مِنَ القَرِينَةِ خَلَا شَرْطُ سَادِسُ ; أَيْ: كَمَا يُمْنَعُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ دُخُولُ المَجَازِ فِيهِ دُونَ ظُهُورِ قَرِينَةٍ، كَذَلِكَ يُمْنَعُ فِيهِ المُشْتَرَكُ دُونَ قَرِينَةٍ تُعَيِّنُ المُرَادَ مِنْهُ؛ كَذِكْرِ العَيْنِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «النَّقْدِ» مَثَلاً . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مَثَلاً) زَادَ لَفْظَةَ: «مَثَلاً» لِيَدْخُلَ نَحْوَ: «الحَيَوَانِ» فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الأَبْيَضِ»، وَإِنَّمَا مَنَعَ ذَلِكَ ; لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ البَيَانُ وَالإِجْمَالُ فِي لَفْظِ المُفْرَدِ يُنَافِيهِ، فَلَوْ كَانَتْ ثَمَّ قَرِينَةٌ مَعَ المُشْتَرَكِ تُبَيِّنُ المُرَادَ مِنْهُ ; كَأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الأَبْيَضِ»: «الحَيَوَانُ المُفَرِّقُ لِلْبَصَرِ»، وَكَأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «النَّقْدِ» : «العَيْنُ أَيْ : النَّضَّارُ» جَازَ، كَمَا قَالَ القَرَّافِيُّ قِيَاساً عَلَى مَا لِلْغَزَالِيِّ فِي المَجَازِ; لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ البَيَانُ، وَقَدْ حَصَلَ.
And his statement: (And no ambiguous word without a context) is the sixth condition; i.e., just as in definition the entry of metaphor is prohibited without the appearance of a context, similarly ambiguous word is prohibited without a context that specifies the intended meaning from it; like mentioning 'عين' in defining 'نقد' for example. And his statement: (for example) he added the word 'for example' to include something like 'الحيوان' in the definition of 'الأبيض'. He prohibited that only because the purpose of definition is elucidation and ambiguity in a single word contradicts it. So if there is a context with the ambiguous word that clarifies the intended meaning from it — such as saying in the definition of 'الأبيض': 'the animal that distinguishes sight', and such as saying in the definition of 'النقد': 'العين i.e. النَّضَّار' — it is permissible, as al-Qarafi said by analogy to what al-Ghazali said concerning metaphor, because the purpose is elucidation, and it has been achieved.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth condition for a valid definition: the definer must not contain an ambiguous word (mushtarak) without a clarifying context (qarina). An ambiguous word has multiple meanings, and using it without context defeats the purpose of definition, which is clarification (bayan). The condition is likened to the prohibition of metaphor in definitions without a context. The example given is using the word 'العين' (which can mean 'eye', 'spring', 'gold coin', or 'essence') in defining 'النقد' (cash or money). Without context, it is unclear which meaning is intended. The text then expands on the word 'مثلاً' (for example) to show that the prohibition applies not only to lexical ambiguous words but also to general words used in a specific sense. For instance, 'الحيوان' (animal) is not ambiguous in itself, but when used in defining 'الأبيض' (white), it is too vague because 'الأبيض' could refer to a white animal or a white thing in general. However, if a clarifying context is added, such as defining 'الأبيض' as 'the animal that distinguishes sight' (referring to the eye), it becomes permissible. Another example: defining 'النقد' as 'العين i.e. النَّضَّار' (the gold coin, i.e. the bright one). This is allowed because the context specifies the intended meaning. Al-Qarafi affirmed this by analogy to al-Ghazali's ruling on metaphor: the purpose of definition is elucidation, and if that is achieved with context, it is permissible.

Key Terms
مشترك
قرينة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is using the word 'العين' without context prohibited in defining 'النقد'?
A) Because 'العين' is a metaphor (مجاز) and metaphors are always prohibited in definitions.
B) Because 'العين' is ambiguous (مشترك) with multiple meanings, and without context it fails to elucidate.
C) Because 'النقد' itself is an ambiguous word.
D) Because al-Ghazali prohibited all uses of 'العين' in definitions.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous words (mushtarak) are forbidden in definitions unless accompanied by a clarifying context (qarina) that specifies the intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the definition of 'wudu' as 'washing specific limbs with water'. Identify if the term 'washing' is ambiguous and what context could clarify it.
?
2. In the definition of 'salah' as 'specified acts of worship with takbir and taslim', check if any word is mushtarak and whether a clarifying context is present.
